The candidate must actively monitor the campaign environment and should not hesitate to report any instances that come to their notice or knowledge involving the publication of election posters or pamphlets that contravene these legal provisions. By reporting such violations to the concerned authorities, candidates contribute to a level playing field and help prevent the spread of misinformation or illegal campaigning tactics. This proactive approach reinforces the principle that elections must be conducted fairly, without the distortion caused by unauthorized or deceptive advertising practices.

In recent times, the Election Commission of India has observed a concerning trend involving the transmission of objectionable messages via Short Message Services, commonly known as SMS, and Interactive Voice Response System calls. These communications are often sent by individuals or groups with vested interests who aim to manipulate public opinion during elections by violating established provisions of election law, the Model Code of Conduct, and specific directions issued by the Commission. Such activities severely vitiate the atmosphere required for free, fair, and peaceful elections, creating confusion and potentially inciting unrest among the electorate. To counteract this, any political advertisements transmitted through bulk SMS or voice messages must undergo a pre-certification process by a designated Media Certification and Monitoring Committee before they can be sent out. The Commission has issued detailed directions to address these challenges effectively. First, regarding objectionable SMS messages that violate election laws or the code of conduct, Police Authorities are instructed to advertise special mobile numbers dedicated to receiving complaints. Recipients of such illicit messages can forward the content along with the sender's phone number to these designated lines. Upon receipt, the Police Authorities must initiate an appropriate inquiry to trace back the original sender of the message. Once identified, the sender faces appropriate legal action under relevant provisions of the Indian Penal Code, the Representation of the People Act of 1951, the Conduct of Election Rules of 1961, and any other applicable laws, ensuring that digital harassment and illegal campaigning are met with swift judicial consequences.

Additionally, the regulatory framework addresses the financial aspects of unauthorized digital campaigning. If bulk SMS messages are transmitted during the campaign period as a form of alternative electioneering, and these are noticed by the Returning Officer or District Election Officer, the matter must be immediately brought to the attention of the Chief Electoral Officer. The Chief Electoral Officer will then investigate the incident to determine the total cost involved by consulting with the telecommunications Service Provider. Once the cost is ascertained, it will be apportioned and added to the election expenditure accounts of the candidate or candidates concerned, depending on the circumstances. This mechanism prevents candidates from benefiting from covert bulk messaging without facing the financial accountability that accompanies traditional advertising methods. Furthermore, to ensure a calm and neutral environment while voters are casting their ballots, there is a strict prohibition on transmitting any bulk SMS or IVRS calls of a political nature. This ban applies for a specific period of forty-eight hours leading up to and ending with the hour fixed for the conclusion of the polling process. This cooling-off period is designed to prevent last-minute inflammatory messages from influencing voters at the critical moment of decision-making, thereby safeguarding the sanctity of the vote.

The requirement for pre-certification of political advertisements stems from a landmark order passed by the Supreme Court on April 13, 2004. This judicial directive mandates that all political advertisements, regardless of whether they are issued by a registered political party, a group of organizations, an association, or an individual contesting candidate, must be certified by the Media Certification and Monitoring Committee before they are telecast or broadcast on television networks, cable channels, or any similar visual media. The scope of this certification is comprehensive and extends beyond traditional broadcasting to include advertisements displayed in cinema halls, those aired on radio stations including private FM channels, audio-visual displays set up in public places, advertisements published in electronic newspapers, the use of bulk SMS and voice messages, as well as advertisements placed on social media platforms and internet websites. This wide-ranging coverage ensures that no medium is left unregulated, closing loopholes that might otherwise be exploited to bypass scrutiny. Moreover, the pre-certification requirement is not limited to electronic media alone; it is equally applicable to print media advertisements during both the pre-poll campaign period and on the actual day of the poll. This uniformity ensures that all forms of political communication are subject to the same standards of legality and ethical compliance before reaching the public.

The administrative machinery for implementing this certification is decentralized to the district level for efficiency. The District Media Certification and Monitoring Committee is the body empowered to entertain applications for the certification of advertisements submitted by an individual or a candidate contesting an election from the specific Parliamentary Constituency in question. It also handles applications from candidates contesting in Assembly Constituencies that fall within the jurisdiction of that Parliamentary Constituency. This localized approach allows for quicker verification and ensures that the committee is familiar with the local context and the candidates involved. Parallel to advertising regulations, the election framework strictly prohibits the practice known as Paid News. Paid News is defined as any news report or analytical piece appearing in any media format, whether print or electronic, where the content has been published in exchange for a price paid in cash or in kind as consideration. Essentially, this refers to disguised advertising presented as impartial journalism, which misleads the electorate by blurring the line between independent reporting and paid promotion. Candidates are strongly advised to refrain from engaging in any Paid News activities, as such practices undermine the credibility of the media and distort the informational landscape of the election. These activities are closely monitored by the Designated Media Certification and Monitoring Committee starting from the date the candidate files their nomination papers.

When a potential case of Paid News is identified, a structured enforcement procedure is triggered. Upon receiving a reference or complaint from the District Media Certification and Monitoring Committee, the Returning Officer issues a formal notice to the candidate involved. This notice must be issued within ninety-six hours of the publication, broadcast, telecast, or receipt of the complaint regarding the alleged paid news item. The notice demands that the candidate either explain and disclose the actual expenditure incurred for publishing the news or similar matter, or provide a justification as to why the expenditure should not be computed based on standard rates. These standard rates are typically derived from the rates approved by the Directorate of Advertising and Visual Publicity or similar government pricing benchmarks. If the candidate fails to reply to this show-cause notice within forty-eight hours of it being served, the decision made by the Media Certification and Monitoring Committee regarding the classification and costing of the material becomes final. If the expenditure is deemed valid and attributable to the candidate, it is added to their total election expenditure account. This rigorous process ensures that candidates cannot hide substantial campaign spending under the guise of favorable news coverage, thereby maintaining financial transparency and preventing the wealthy from gaining an unfair advantage through covert media purchases. The entire system is designed to uphold the purity of elections by ensuring that all communication with the voter is transparent, authorized, and accounted for within the legal limits prescribed by the Constitution and election statutes.

In the intricate framework of electoral administration, a structured mechanism exists for candidates to challenge administrative decisions regarding their eligibility or conduct. Should a candidate receive an adverse final decision from the District level office of the election management body, they possess the right to appeal this ruling to the State level authority. This appeal must be lodged within a strict timeframe of forty-eight hours following the receipt of the original decision, and it is mandatory that the District level office be formally informed of this escalation. The hierarchy of recourse continues upward; if the candidate remains dissatisfied with the outcome of the State level review, they may further appeal to the central Election Commission. This secondary appeal also operates under a stringent forty-eight-hour deadline, calculated from the moment the candidate receives the order issued by the State level body. This tiered system ensures that grievances are addressed swiftly and at the appropriate administrative level while maintaining the integrity of the election timeline.

Regarding the digital landscape of modern campaigning, specific guidelines govern the use of social media to ensure transparency and accountability. Candidates are legally obligated to disclose the details of their authentic social media accounts during the nomination filing process, specifically utilizing a designated form known as Form-26. This requirement allows election officials to monitor official digital communications effectively. Furthermore, financial transparency is paramount in the digital age. Candidates and political parties must meticulously record all expenditures related to social media campaigning within their official election expenditure accounts. This comprehensive financial reporting includes costs incurred for political advertisements placed on these platforms, expenses for maintaining the accounts, fees paid for content development, and even the salaries of employees hired specifically to manage these digital presences. By treating digital operations as tangible campaign costs, the regulations prevent the concealment of spending through online channels.

The ethical standards governing traditional campaigning extend fully into the virtual realm. The provisions of the Model Code of Conduct, which serves as a moral compass for fair play during elections, along with all related instructions, apply equally to any content posted on social media platforms by candidates and political parties. This ensures that the same rules against hate speech, misinformation, and divisive rhetoric online as they do offline. A critical technical distinction is made regarding the classification of social media content. By definition, social media falls under the broader category of electronic media. Consequently, any political advertisement disseminated through social media channels is subject to pre-certification requirements. This means that before such advertisements can be published, they must be reviewed and approved by the relevant authorities to ensure compliance with legal and ethical standards. However, a notable exception exists for organic content. Political messages, comments, photographs, or videos that are posted or uploaded directly by candidates or parties onto their own blogs or personal website accounts are not classified as political advertisements. Therefore, such self-published content does not require pre-certification, distinguishing between paid or formal advertising and direct communication from the candidate to the public.

As the election cycle nears its conclusion, strict restrictions are imposed on the physical presence of political figures within constituencies to preserve the sanctity of the voting period. Section 126 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, explicitly prohibits active campaigning during the forty-eight-hour period leading up to the close of polls. To enforce this silence period, the Election Commission has issued directives requiring district election and police administrations to ensure that all political functionaries vacate the constituency immediately after the official campaign period ends. This measure is designed to prevent last-minute influence on voters. However, a pragmatic exception has been established for elected representatives. An incumbent Member of Parliament or Member of the Legislative Assembly representing the area undergoing election is not required to leave their constituency, even if they are not registered voters in that specific polling segment. Despite being allowed to remain in their residence or official capacity, these elected officials are strictly forbidden from engaging in any campaign activities within the constituency during the forty-eight-hour blackout period. Additionally, in the context of Parliamentary Constituency elections, a Member of the Legislative Assembly is restricted to staying only within their own specific assembly segment and is prohibited from visiting other assembly segments that comprise the larger Parliamentary Constituency, thereby preventing cross-segment campaigning during the sensitive pre-poll window.

To assist voters in navigating the logistical aspects of casting their ballot, political parties and candidates are permitted to distribute unofficial identity slips. These documents serve purely as informational aids and must contain only specific, neutral data: the name and serial number of the voter as listed in the electoral roll, the part number of the electoral roll, and the serial number and name of the assigned polling station. The design and content of these slips are heavily regulated to prevent them from becoming tools of persuasion. They must be printed on plain white paper and are strictly prohibited from displaying the name of the candidate, the name of the political party, or the party's election symbol. Furthermore, these slips cannot contain any slogans or exhortations urging voters to support a particular party or candidate. Including such persuasive language would constitute canvassing, which is illegal within the vicinity of a polling station. The law defines a prohibited zone extending two hundred meters around the polling station; circulating any slip containing slogans or appeals within this radius is considered unlawful canvassing. The prescribed format for these slips includes fields for the Parliamentary or Assembly Constituency name, the polling station number and name along with location details, the voter's serial number in the roll, the part number, and the voter's name, ensuring the document remains a functional guide rather than a campaign tool.

Preparation for the actual voting process involves several rehearsal and educational mechanisms designed to familiarize candidates and voters with the procedures. Candidates have the right to arrange for their appointed polling agents to attend polling rehearsals conducted in their respective areas. These sessions are crucial for agents to gain hands-on familiarity with the entire polling procedure, including the setup of the polling booth, the verification of voters, and the technical operation of voting machines. This ensures that the candidate's representatives can effectively monitor the process and address any procedural irregularities on election day. In addition to agent training, candidates are allowed to print dummy ballot papers for educational purposes. These sample ballots may feature the candidate's own name and symbol in the exact position where they will appear on the official ballot, helping voters understand how to cast their vote correctly. However, to avoid confusion or misrepresentation, these dummy papers must not display the names or symbols of any other contesting candidates in the constituency. Visual distinctions are also mandated; dummy ballot papers may be printed in colors such as brown, yellow, or grey, but they are strictly forbidden from using pink or white, which are reserved for official documents. Furthermore, the size and overall appearance of the dummy ballot must not resemble the genuine ballot paper, ensuring no voter mistakes the sample for the real thing.

Complementing the dummy ballot papers, candidates and political parties are also permitted to create dummy Balloting Units to educate the electorate on how to operate the voting machinery. These mock units serve as practical training tools, allowing voters to practice pressing the correct button corresponding to their chosen candidate before entering the actual voting booth. These dummy units can be constructed from materials such as wood, plastic, or plywood and are typically built to be half the size of the official Balloting Units to clearly distinguish them from government property. Like the dummy ballots, these mock machines should be painted in non-official colors such as brown, yellow, or grey. They are equipped to display the serial number, name, and symbol of the candidate, mirroring the layout of the actual machine but remaining distinctly different in scale and coloration. This proactive approach to voter education aims to reduce errors, minimize spoiled votes, and ensure that every voter can confidently and correctly exercise their franchise without intimidation or confusion caused by unfamiliar technology.

The electronic voting machine system is designed with specific user interface elements to ensure clarity and accessibility for all voters, including those who may not be able to read. One such feature involves the integration of a battery-operated button mechanism accompanied by an indicator lamp. When a voter presses the button corresponding to their chosen candidate, the lamp illuminates to provide immediate visual confirmation that the vote has been successfully recorded. This tactile and visual feedback loop is crucial for building trust in the electronic process, ensuring that every individual, regardless of literacy level, can verify that their intent has been captured by the machine. To further support this goal, particularly for illiterate voters who might find the technology intimidating or confusing, the Election Commission has issued comprehensive instructions regarding the use of printed samples of the Electronic Voting Machine, often referred to as EVMs, at polling stations. These guidelines mandate that a physical, real-size model of the EVM Ballot Unit be created and supplied to every presiding officer in charge of a polling station. This model is typically mounted on a sturdy cardboard backing to simulate the actual device found inside the voting compartment. Great care is taken during the production of these educational models to ensure that they do not cause confusion or inadvertently favor any political party. Consequently, the printed sample must utilize only dummy names and dummy symbols that are not currently in use by any registered political party. It is strictly prohibited to print real candidate names or actual party symbols on these training aids. Furthermore, the printing process must be done in full color to accurately represent the operational indicators of the machine, such as the blue button used for casting a vote, the green light that signals a successful vote registration, and the red light that indicates the machine is ready or busy. This attention to detail ensures that the visual cues on the model match the real equipment perfectly, allowing for effective demonstration.

The protocol for using these cardboard models is strictly defined to maintain the secrecy and integrity of the ballot while providing necessary assistance. Whenever a voter approaches the polling staff expressing difficulty or explicitly asking for help on how to operate the EVM, the presiding officer is authorized to use the cardboard model to explain the voting process. The explanation must be conducted in a manner that ensures the voter fully understands the steps required to cast their ballot. However, a critical constraint is placed on where this demonstration can occur. The instruction of the voter using the model must take place outside the private voting compartment and must always be conducted in the presence of polling agents who represent the various candidates. This transparency prevents any allegation of coercion or undue influence. Under no circumstances is the presiding officer or any polling staff permitted to enter the voting compartment with the voter to demonstrate the machine, nor should they frequent the compartment unnecessarily, as such behavior could create an environment ripe for complaints regarding the violation of voter privacy. Additionally, to safeguard the physical integrity of the Ballot Unit, the presiding officer is tasked with periodically inspecting the machine to ensure that no voter has attempted to tamper with it by pasting papers, tapes, or other materials over the candidate names, symbols, or ballot buttons. Such inspections, however, must be carried out with extreme caution to avoid disrupting the voting process or violating secrecy. Therefore, any inspection of the Ballot Unit must be performed only when there is no voter inside the voting compartment and must be done in the immediate presence of the polling agents to serve as witnesses to the integrity of the inspection.

Regarding the use of political symbols during the election campaign, specific rules are established to balance voter convenience with the prevention of malpractice within the polling station. Candidates are permitted to print copies of their assigned election symbols and distribute them to the public as part of their campaigning efforts. This helps voters, especially those who rely on symbol recognition rather than text, to identify their preferred candidate. However, a strict prohibition exists regarding the entry of these materials into the polling area. While voters may possess these symbol cards prior to arriving at the polling station, they are absolutely forbidden from carrying any paper containing the symbol into the polling station itself. This rule is enforced to prevent voters from using such papers as guides inside the voting compartment, which could compromise the secrecy of the ballot if observed by others, or potentially be used to prove how they voted, thereby opening the door to vote-buying or coercion. The focus remains on ensuring that the act of voting is based on memory and personal choice made in isolation, without external aids that could be scrutinized by third parties.

In recent times, the Election Commission of India has identified a significant challenge concerning electoral fraud involving impersonation. Observations have shown that on the day of the poll, individuals sometimes attempt to cast votes in the names of voters who have passed away or have permanently moved to different locations. This issue often comes to light during the intense period of canvassing, where workers and agents of candidates interact closely with the local community. These campaign workers may discover that certain names listed in the official electoral roll belong to individuals who are deceased or who have left the locality either temporarily or permanently. To address this vulnerability, candidates are encouraged to proactively compile a list of such dead, absentee, shifted, and duplicate voters for each polling station within their constituency. The ideal scenario involves collaboration among all contesting candidates to agree upon a unified list of these questionable entries. If such consensus can be reached, this agreed-upon list should be delivered to the Returning Officer, the senior official responsible for conducting the election in that constituency, at least seven days before the first day of polling. However, recognizing that political rivals may not always agree, the guidelines provide a fallback mechanism. Even if a unanimous list cannot be generated, candidates should strive to get as many opponents as possible to agree to the entries. Failing that, a candidate is still empowered to supply their own independent list of suspected dead or shifted voters to the Returning Officer, ensuring that the authorities are aware of potential fraud vectors before the election begins.

To actively prevent impersonation during the polling hours, the Commission has issued rigorous directions specifically targeting the category of Absentee, Shifted, and Dead electors, often abbreviated as ASD voters. A specialized list of these ASD voters must be prepared separately for each polling station, and it is mandatory that every Presiding Officer receives a distinct copy of this list relevant to their specific station. On the day of the poll, the standard voting procedure is modified for any elector whose name appears on this ASD list. Unlike regular voters who may only need to show their identity card, an elector found on the ASD list must personally produce a prescribed identification document to prove their living status and identity. The Presiding Officer is required to personally verify this identification document with heightened scrutiny. Once verified, the details of this transaction must be properly registered by the concerned Polling Officer in the official register of voters, known as Form 17A. This form serves as the legal record of every vote cast. An additional layer of security is applied to the signing process for these voters. In addition to providing their signature in the designated column of Form 17A, these electors must also provide a thumb impression. This requirement is absolute and applies even to literate electors who are fully capable of signing their names; the thumb impression is mandatory in addition to the signature, not as a substitute. This dual verification method creates a robust biometric and written record that makes it significantly harder for an imposter to succeed.

The accountability for managing these special cases extends beyond the moment of voting. The Presiding Officer is obligated to maintain a detailed record of all such incidents and must issue a certificate in a prescribed format at the conclusion of the polling day. This certificate, which states the exact number of electors from the ASD list who were allowed to vote after proper scrutiny, must be kept attached to Form 17A for future scrutiny and audit purposes. This ensures that there is a clear paper trail for every exceptional vote cast. Furthermore, in polling stations where videography or photography is being conducted as part of the observation process, specific provisions apply to these ASD voters. Any elector voting from this list must be photographed, and this visual record must be preserved alongside their documentation. This photographic evidence serves as an undeniable proof of the person who actually cast the vote, deterring impersonators who might otherwise rely on forged documents. The role of Micro observers, who are independent officials deployed to monitor the fairness of the election, is also critical in this context. These observers must meticulously ensure that all instructions regarding Absentee, Shifted, and Dead electors are followed to the letter. They are required to make a specific mention of the handling of ASD voters in their official reports, noting any deviations or strict adherence to the protocol. Finally, to guarantee that these complex procedures are executed correctly, the Presiding Officer must be specially briefed before the poll begins. This briefing focuses specifically on the procedures to be followed for electors appearing on the ASD list, ensuring that the officer in charge is fully aware of the enhanced verification steps, the necessity of the dual signature and thumb impression, and the documentation requirements, thereby minimizing the risk of human error in preventing electoral fraud.

The Election Commission has issued specific directives regarding the identification of overseas electors at the time they cast their votes at a polling station. To ensure the integrity and accuracy of the voting process for citizens residing abroad, the identification of these individuals must be conducted exclusively based on the original passports they produce. This requirement eliminates ambiguity and ensures that only valid, government-issued travel documents are accepted as proof of identity and nationality. Furthermore, procedural rigor is maintained through the handling of voter slips. The voter slip collected from each overseas voter must be tagged in a serial order to maintain a clear chain of custody and record-keeping. Once the polling process is completed for the day, these serially tagged slips must not be mixed with other general election materials but should instead be secured in a separate envelope specifically provided for this purpose. This segregation of documents facilitates easier auditing and verification processes post-election, ensuring that the votes of overseas citizens are accounted for distinctly and transparently.

Great care must be taken in the preparation of various electoral lists, particularly those categorizing voters who are deceased, absent, have shifted residence, or are identified as duplicate entries. The accuracy of these lists is paramount because any error can lead to significant administrative and legal complications. Specifically, officials must ensure that the name of no genuine, living voter is incorrectly included in these lists of invalid or non-participating voters. If a real voter is wrongly categorized as dead, absent, or a duplicate, it will cause considerable annoyance and distress to that individual. More critically, such an error could result in their fundamental right to vote being questioned or denied when they arrive at the polling station. This scenario not only undermines the voter's confidence in the electoral system but also creates unnecessary confrontations at the polling booth, disrupting the smooth conduct of the election. Therefore, the compilation of these lists requires meticulous verification against official records to prevent the disenfranchisement of eligible citizens.

To further safeguard the election against fraudulent activities, candidates are instructed to take proactive measures by providing their polling agents with copies of the lists containing names of dead, absentee, shifted, and duplicate bogus voters relevant to each specific polling station. By handing over these lists, the candidate empowers their agent to remain vigilant on polling day. The agent can then closely monitor the stream of voters to watch for any person attempting to cast a ballot in the name of someone listed as deceased or otherwise ineligible. If the polling agent observes such an attempt, they are authorized and expected to immediately draw the attention of the Presiding Officer to the fact. This collaborative approach between the candidate, their agent, and the election authorities serves as a critical check against impersonation and bogus voting. By diligently following these instructions, a candidate not only protects their own electoral interests but also materially assists the election authorities in maintaining the purity of the electoral roll and preventing illegal voting practices.

The legal framework governing elections also addresses the serious and sensitive issue of the death of a candidate before the polling takes place. Historically, the law under Section 52 of the Representation of the People Act of 1951 provided for the countermanding, or complete cancellation, of an election upon the death of any candidate. However, this provision underwent a significant transformation with the enactment of the Representation of the People Amendment Act in 1996. Under the amended Section 52, the rules are now more specific and restrictive. The poll shall only be adjourned, meaning postponed to a later date rather than cancelled entirely, in the event of the death of a candidate who was set up by a recognised political party, and only if specific circumstances are met. These circumstances are strictly defined to ensure clarity and prevent misuse of the provision.

The first circumstance under which a poll may be adjourned is if the candidate dies at any time after 11:00 a.m. on the last date designated for making nominations, provided that their nomination has subsequently been found valid during the scrutiny process conducted under Section 36 of the Act. The second circumstance involves a situation where the candidate's nomination has already been validated under Section 36, and they have not withdrawn their candidature under Section 37, but they pass away before the list of contesting candidates is published under Section 38. In this case, a report of the death must be received before that publication occurs. The third circumstance applies if the candidate dies after becoming a contesting candidate, meaning they are officially on the ballot, and a report of their death is received before the actual commencement of the poll. In any of these three scenarios, the Returning Officer, upon being satisfied with the factual evidence regarding the death of the candidate, is empowered to order the adjournment of the poll. The new date for the rescheduled poll will then be notified later by the Election Commission.

There are additional procedural nuances that must be observed, particularly concerning the timing of scrutiny and the status of the political party. In cases where the candidate dies after the nomination deadline but before the poll, the order to adjourn the poll will only be made after the scrutiny of all nominations has been completed, including the nomination of the deceased candidate. It is crucial to note that for the purpose of triggering an adjournment, the candidate shall be deemed to have been set up by a recognised political party only if all the requirements mentioned in paragraph 13 of the Election Symbols Reservation and Allotment Order of 1968 have been fully fulfilled by the time of the candidate's death. This includes the submission of specific intimation forms, known as Form A and Form B, by the political party. If the party has not provided this required intimation in the prescribed forms by the time the candidate passes away, the candidate will not be considered as having been set up by a recognised political party for the purpose of adjourning the poll. Consequently, any contention by a political party that they would have furnished the required information by 3:00 p.m. on that day, as technically allowed under paragraph 13 of the Symbols Order, cannot be accepted if the death occurred prior to that submission. The timeline is absolute and tied to the moment of death.

Another vital distinction in this legal context relates to the definition of a recognised political party. For the purposes of Section 52 of the Representation of the People Act of 1951, the term recognised political party refers strictly to a party that is recognised as a National party or a party that is recognised as a State Party within the specific State where the election is taking place. This geographic specificity is essential. A political party that is recognised as a State Party in one particular State will be treated merely as a registered but unrecognised party in any other State where it does not hold State Party status. This classification holds true even if that party has been granted concessions to use its unique symbol in other States for campaign purposes. Therefore, if a candidate set up by such a party, which lacks State Party recognition in the specific election jurisdiction, passes away, the poll will not be adjourned. The election proceedings will continue as scheduled, highlighting the importance of the party's official recognition status in the local context.

Finally, the procedure following the confirmation of a candidate's death involves active intervention by the Election Commission to ensure the democratic process continues. Upon receipt of the report from the Returning Officer confirming the death of a candidate who was set up by a recognised political party, the Election Commission will formally call upon the concerned political party to nominate a replacement candidate for the said election. This step ensures that the party retains its opportunity to contest the seat despite the tragic loss of their original nominee. The political party is obligated to make this new nomination within seven days of the issuance of the notice by the Election Commission. This seven-day window provides a structured timeframe for the party to select a new representative, thereby minimizing delays in the electoral calendar while respecting the need for a fair and complete contest. This comprehensive framework balances the need for procedural stability with the flexibility required to handle unforeseen tragedies during the election cycle.

The procedural framework for substituting a candidate in the event of unforeseen circumstances, such as the death of an original nominee, mandates a rigorous adherence to established administrative protocols to ensure the integrity of the electoral process. When a new individual is put forward to replace a deceased candidate, this nomination must be executed using the specific statutory forms designated for such purposes, known formally as Form 2A or Form 2B, depending on the specific context of the election. This requirement ensures that the documentation aligns precisely with legal standards and allows for uniform processing by election officials. Furthermore, the substitute candidate is not exempt from financial obligations inherent to the candidacy; they are required to furnish a fresh security deposit. This deposit serves as a formal commitment and a safeguard against frivolous nominations, ensuring that only serious contenders participate in the electoral fray. In addition to the personal nomination forms, the submission must include official communication from the political party to which the candidate belongs. This takes the form of mandatory intimations documented in Form A and Form B, as prescribed under paragraph 13 of the Election Symbols Reservation and Allotment Order of 1968. These forms are critical because they legally verify the party's endorsement of the new candidate and secure the allocation of the party's reserved symbol, which is vital for voter recognition. Beyond these specific substitution requirements, the entire spectrum of existing laws governing the nomination process remains fully applicable. This includes the detailed scrutiny of nomination papers by returning officers to validate eligibility, as well as the provisions allowing for the withdrawal of candidature within the legally permitted timeframe. Every step must mirror the procedures followed for initial candidates to maintain fairness and legal consistency.

Regarding the eligibility of individuals who have previously withdrawn from the race, the law provides a specific exception in cases where a replacement is needed due to a candidate's demise. A person who had already submitted a formal notice of withdrawal of their candidature under the relevant subsection of section 37 prior to the adjournment of the poll retains the right to be renominated. This provision acknowledges that a voluntary withdrawal made before the crisis occurred should not permanently bar a capable individual from serving as a replacement when the situation demands it. Once such a nomination is accepted and processed, the administrative machinery must update the public record to reflect this change. If the list of contesting candidates had already been officially published under section 38 before the poll was adjourned, the Returning Officer is duty-bound to prepare and publish a completely revised list. This new document will incorporate the name of the newly nominated candidate, effectively replacing the deceased individual, and must be disseminated to ensure that voters, polling agents, and other stakeholders are aware of the current roster of contestants. Following these administrative updates, the election proceeds according to a revised schedule issued by the Election Commission. This adjusted timeline accounts for the time lost due to the adjournment and the additional days required for the fresh nomination and scrutiny processes, ensuring that all subsequent stages of the election, from campaigning to voting and counting, are conducted in an orderly and synchronized manner.

To uphold the transparency and fairness of elections, the Election Commission has instituted a comprehensive regime of videography to monitor critical events throughout the electoral cycle. The primary objective of this initiative is to create an authentic, faithful, and simultaneous record of any violations of election laws and standing instructions. By capturing visual evidence, authorities can accurately assess the impact of corrective measures and hold violators accountable. This mandate covers a broad spectrum of activities, extending beyond just the day of voting to include the entire period of public campaigning, the secure transport and receipt of Electronic Voting Machines and Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail devices, the intricate process of vote counting, and the final declaration of results. The approach is designed to be independent, intelligent, and purposeful, meaning that recording is not random but strategically focused on areas prone to malpractice. Specific scenarios flagged for special surveillance through video cameras or closed-circuit television systems include public meetings addressed or attended by high-ranking government ministers and top leaders of recognized political parties, as these gatherings often draw large crowds and carry higher risks of code violations. Additionally, any signs of civil unrest, such as riots, commotions, stone-throwing incidents, or chaotic free-for-all situations, are prioritized for recording to facilitate swift law enforcement intervention.

The scope of videographic monitoring also extends to violent incidents that threaten the sanctity of the election, including damage to public or private property, looting, arson, and the open brandishing of weapons. Particular attention is paid to the serious offense of booth capturing, where unauthorized individuals seize control of a polling station to manipulate votes, as well as any attempts to intimidate voters or coerce them into voting a certain way. The system also targets subtle forms of corruption, such as the inducement or bribing of voters through the distribution of material goods like sarees, dhotis, or blankets, which are illegal under election expenditure laws. Enforcement teams monitor for illegal canvassing activities occurring within the prohibited zone of one hundred meters surrounding polling stations, ensuring a neutral environment for voters. Extravagant displays of wealth, such as the erection of massive cut-outs or other vulgar exhibitions of campaign spending, are also documented to enforce expenditure caps. Furthermore, the movements and activities of candidates with doubtful backgrounds or criminal records are tracked closely to prevent potential disruptions. Key administrative milestones, including the filing of nominations, the scrutiny of papers, and the withdrawal of candidatures, are recorded, as are the technical preparations of Electronic Voting Machines by Returning Officers. The security chain of custody is visually verified by filming the closure of strong rooms after machines are deposited and their subsequent opening before counting begins, culminating in full coverage of the counting process itself. It is important to note that this list of monitored events is illustrative rather than exhaustive, allowing officials the discretion to record any other suspicious activity. To ensure the evidentiary value of these recordings, all video and digital photography must embed accurate date and time stamps, enabling authorities to verify the exact moment an incident occurred.

At the micro-level of the polling station, critical events in and around the facility are captured using video cameras, digital cameras, or CCTV systems to guarantee procedural compliance. Examples of such localized monitoring include the mock poll conducted before the actual voting begins to demonstrate that the machines are functioning correctly, followed by the sealing of the Electronic Voting Machines and Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail units. The correct positioning of voting compartments is recorded to ensure voter privacy, while the presence of authorized polling agents is verified to maintain oversight. Cameras also document the situation at the close of the scheduled polling hours, specifically capturing voters waiting in line and ensuring that the last voter in the queue is allowed to cast their ballot. Visits by sector officers, election observers, and other electoral functionaries are also filmed to record their inspection activities. Once these video recordings are prepared, they are immediately reviewed by the Returning Officer. This prompt examination allows for the rapid identification of any organizers, speakers, or participants in public meetings who may have committed violations of statutory provisions, directions issued by the Commission, or the Model Code of Conduct. Upon identifying guilt through this visual evidence, immediate corrective action is taken, which may include disciplinary measures against those found responsible, thereby reinforcing the rule of law and deterring future infractions. This robust framework of visual documentation serves as both a deterrent against misconduct and a tool for justice, ensuring that the democratic process remains free, fair, and transparent.

The Electronic Voting Machine, commonly referred to as the EVM, is a sophisticated electronic device designed to facilitate the casting and recording of votes in an election. This system is fundamentally composed of two distinct but interconnected units: the Control Unit and the Ballot Unit. These two components are linked together by a specialized cable that measures five meters in length, allowing for flexible placement within the voting booth while maintaining a secure physical connection. The Ballot Unit serves as the interface for the voter, featuring buttons corresponding to each candidate. A single Ballot Unit is engineered to accommodate up to sixteen candidates. In scenarios where an election contest involves a larger number of participants, the system allows for scalability through a process known as cascading. Up to twenty-four Ballot Units can be connected in a series to a single Control Unit, thereby enabling the machine to handle a maximum of three hundred and eighty-four candidates, a figure that includes the option for None of the Above, or NOTA. The entire apparatus is designed to be portable and self-sufficient, operating on a dedicated power pack or battery that supplies seven point five volts, ensuring functionality even in areas with unreliable electricity grids. To ensure inclusivity and accessibility for all citizens, particularly those with visual impairments, the design incorporates tactile guidance features. On the right-hand side of the Ballot Unit, adjacent to the buttons used to cast a vote for each candidate, the numbers one through sixteen are embossed in Braille signage. This critical feature allows visually impaired electors to independently identify and select their preferred candidate without assistance, preserving the secrecy and dignity of their vote.

Complementing the electronic recording mechanism is the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail, known as the VVPAT, which functions as an independent verification system attached to the Electronic Voting Machines. The primary purpose of the VVPAT is to provide a physical record of the vote cast, allowing voters to verify that their intention has been accurately captured by the electronic system. This unit operates on its own dedicated power pack, requiring a higher voltage of twenty-two point five volts to drive the printing mechanism. During the voting process, the spatial arrangement of these components is strictly regulated to maintain security and order. The Control Unit, which acts as the brain of the operation and controls the flow of votes, is kept under the direct supervision of the Presiding Officer or Polling Officer outside the immediate voting compartment. Conversely, the Ballot Unit and the VVPAT printer are situated inside the voting compartment, ensuring that the voter interacts with the selection interface in privacy. When a voter presses a button to cast their vote, the system triggers a sequence of events. First, a paper slip is printed by the VVPAT printer containing specific details: the serial number, the name, and the symbol of the candidate for whom the vote was cast. This slip remains visible to the voter through a transparent glass window for a duration of approximately seven seconds, providing ample time for verification. After this brief exposure period, the machine automatically cuts the slip, and it drops into a sealed drop box within the VVPAT unit, where it is stored securely for potential future audits. Simultaneously, an audible beep sound is emitted from the Control Unit, serving as an immediate confirmation to the polling officer and the voter that the vote has been successfully registered in the electronic memory of the EVM.

The management of these critical devices involves rigorous protocols regarding their storage, movement, and the security of the facilities housing them. Various types of storage configurations are defined to suit different phases of the election cycle. An EVM Warehouse refers to a designated building or set of rooms specifically constructed or assigned for storing EVMs and VVPATs during periods when no elections are taking place. These warehouses are typically located at District Headquarters or, in exceptional cases with valid justification, at Tehsil Headquarters. In contrast, an EVM Strong Room is a designated hall or room used for storing these machines during the active election period, specifically from the time of the First Level Checking until the completion of the election petition filing period. There are several categories of Strong Rooms, each serving a specific function, including the FLC Strong Room for initial checks, Repair Strong Room for maintenance, Training and Awareness Strong Room for educational purposes, AC or AS Strong Room for accounts, Polled Strong Room for machines that have already been used, Reserve Strong Room for backups, and the District Strong Room for central custody. The physical infrastructure of these warehouses and strong rooms is subject to stringent standards to prevent tampering, environmental damage, or unauthorized access. A fundamental requirement is that the facility must have only one entry and exit point, ensuring that all movement can be monitored and controlled effectively. All other doors and windows in the warehouse are permanently sealed using brick-masonry or concrete to eliminate any possibility of breach. Furthermore, the main electrical switch for the facility is installed outside the warehouse or strong room and is disconnected after the sealing process to prevent any internal electrical manipulation.

Environmental control within these storage facilities is paramount to preserving the integrity and longevity of the electronic equipment. The premises must be completely free from dampness, pests, rodents, and any risk of flooding or water logging. The structure should also be devoid of cracks or leakage that could compromise the interior environment. To ensure proper air circulation and prevent the buildup of heat or humidity, an exhaust fan is installed on the front side of the building at the highest feasible point. This ventilation opening is protected by a strong iron grill to prevent unauthorized entry while allowing airflow. Strict inventory control is enforced within these spaces; no materials other than those prescribed by the Election Commission, whether related to elections or not, are permitted to be stored alongside the EVMs and VVPATs. A specific regulation mandates that EVMs belonging to State Election Commissions or those loaned to a State Election Commission shall never be stored in a warehouse designated for EVMs belonging to the Election Commission of India, ensuring clear jurisdictional separation. Before any EVMs are stored in a newly constructed warehouse or any existing facility, the District Election Officer is required to obtain a formal certificate from the concerned authority. This certificate verifies that the quality and physical aspects of the warehouse meet all necessary standards, and this document is kept on record for future reference and accountability.

Security and safety arrangements for these warehouses are equally comprehensive, employing a multi-layered approach to deter and detect any potential threats. A double lock system is mandatory for all storage areas, with the keys for each of the two locks held by two separate specified officers, ensuring that no single individual can access the facility alone. Continuous protection is provided by a minimum of half a section of armed police personnel who maintain a twenty-four hours a day, seven days a week security presence. This security detail must consist exclusively of government security personnel, although Home Guards may be deployed as an exception in situations where regular police forces cannot be posted. Technological surveillance is integrated through the installation of Closed Circuit Television cameras positioned to capture the hall door and the corridor. These cameras are connected to a Digital Video Recorder that stores footage for a minimum of thirty days, providing a retrievable history of all activities near the entrance. Administrative controls include the maintenance of a log book for recording every entry and exit, as well as a duty roster for security personnel to ensure shift continuity. Videography is conducted at the precise moments of opening and closing the warehouse, and these procedures must be carried out in the presence of representatives from national and state-recognized political parties or candidates to ensure transparency. Additionally, adequate fire safety measures and a functional fire alarm system are installed to protect the valuable electronic assets from fire hazards.

Before every General Election or Bye-election for Assembly and Parliamentary Constituencies, a critical procedure known as First Level Checking, or FLC, is conducted on all EVMs and VVPATs. This process is executed by authorized engineers from established public sector entities such as Bharat Electronics Limited or the Electronics Corporation of India Limited. The checking takes place at the district level and is carried out under the direct supervision of the District Election Officer to ensure adherence to protocol. Transparency is a cornerstone of this phase; the FLC is performed in the presence of representatives from national and state-recognized political parties. The entire process is covered by CCTV cameras and is thoroughly documented to create an auditable trail. The FLC process itself is meticulous and involves several stages. It begins with cleaning and a detailed visual inspection of the machines to identify any physical defects or signs of tampering. This is followed by functionality checks using a specialized device known as the P-FLCU, which tests the full operational capabilities of the unit. A crucial component of the FLC is the conduct of a mock poll. For each individual EVM, comprising both the Ballot Unit and Control Unit, and for each VVPAT, a mock poll is conducted by casting six votes against each of the sixteen candidate buttons. The results are observed to ensure accuracy, and subsequently, the mock poll data is cleared from the machine's memory.

To further validate the reliability of the machines through statistical sampling, additional extensive mock polls are performed on a percentage basis relative to the quantity of Control Units that have passed the initial FLC OK status. Specifically, a mock poll involving twelve hundred votes is conducted on one percent of the total EVMs and VVPATs. Furthermore, a mock poll of one thousand votes is carried out on two percent of the machines, and a mock poll of five hundred votes is executed on another two percent of the units. These extensive testing procedures are all conducted in the presence of the representatives of political parties to maintain trust and openness. A vital verification step during these mock polls involves tallying the electronic count recorded by the Control Unit with the physical count of the slips generated by the VVPAT. This cross-verification ensures that the electronic recording mechanism and the paper audit trail are perfectly synchronized, confirming that the vote counted electronically matches the vote printed on paper. The percentages for these sample sizes are calculated based on the total number of Control Units that have been declared fit after the initial checking, ensuring that the sample size is representative of the actual pool of machines ready for deployment in the upcoming election.

The rigorous process of validating electronic voting machines begins with a specialized load test designed to ensure the absolute reliability of equipment that has successfully passed its First Level Check, commonly referred to as FLC-OK status. In this critical quality assurance phase, a statistically significant sample comprising one percent of these verified machines is randomly selected for intensive stress testing. The technical configuration for this test involves connecting four distinct Ballot Units, which are the interfaces where voters cast their votes, to a single Control Unit, the brain of the system that records and stores the data, along with one Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail machine, which produces a physical paper record of each vote. During this simulation, the system is subjected to a heavy workload by casting at least one vote for every candidate button available on the connected Ballot Units, resulting in a minimum of sixty-four total votes being processed in a single cycle. Following this intense usage, election officials meticulously tally the count of the physical paper slips generated by the VVPAT machine against the digital count stored in the Control Unit to verify that the electronic recording matches the physical evidence perfectly, ensuring no discrepancies exist under load conditions. It is a fundamental rule of the electoral framework that only those Electronic Voting Machines and VVPAT units that have successfully cleared this First Level Check and subsequent verification protocols are permitted for use in actual elections, and a comprehensive list of these certified machines is transparently shared with all recognized political parties to maintain trust and openness in the process. To safeguard the integrity of these devices from the moment they are certified until Election Day, a robust security protocol is enforced wherein one full section of armed police personnel is deployed to provide round-the-clock, twenty-four-seven protection, ensuring that the machines remain secure from any potential tampering or unauthorized access throughout the entire pre-election period.

In addition to the machines designated for actual voting, a specific portion of the inventory is allocated for educational purposes to ensure that all stakeholders understand the technology. For the explicit purpose of Training and Awareness, District Election Officers are authorized to withdraw a subset of Electronic Voting Machines from the main pool, adhering to a strict upper limit that ensures no more than ten percent of the total number of polling stations in a district are affected by this withdrawal. To prevent any confusion between machines used for practice and those destined for the ballot, a distinctive yellow-colored sticker bearing the inscription Training or Awareness is firmly affixed to both the electronic units themselves and their protective carrying cases, serving as a clear visual indicator of their non-operational status for the upcoming election. Transparency remains paramount even in this training phase, as the detailed list of these specific EVMs and VVPATs set aside for educational drills is shared with both National and State level recognized political parties. These training units are not permanently removed from the electoral ecosystem; rather, they serve as a flexible reserve that may be re-introduced into the active pool should any shortfall arise later in the process. If needed, these units undergo a fresh First Level Check and a supplementary randomization process before being commissioned again. At the time of final commissioning, after passing these renewed checks and randomization steps, these previously designated training units are formally integrated into the inventory as reserve EVMs and VVPATs, ready to replace any malfunctioning units on election day.

The allocation of these verified machines to specific geographic areas is managed through a sophisticated, multi-stage randomization process designed to eliminate any possibility of predictability or manipulation. The first stage, known as the First Randomization, is executed using the specialized EVM Management System, a secure software platform developed by the Election Commission of India specifically for this purpose. This initial distribution is conducted by the District Election Officer in the physical presence of representatives from recognized political parties to ensure observational oversight. The objective of this stage is to allocate the pool of EVMs and VVPATs to various Assembly Constituencies or segments based on the administrative divisions of the region, after which the allocated machines are handed over to the concerned Returning Officer who manages that specific constituency. Once this list is finalized, it is disseminated to National and State recognized political parties, and subsequently to the individual candidates once the official list of contesting candidates has been confirmed, allowing all participants to know which batch of machines has been assigned to their area without knowing which specific machine will go to which polling station.

Following the confirmation of candidates, a second layer of randomness is applied to further secure the electoral process. The Second Randomization takes place after the list of contesting candidates is finalized and immediately prior to the final commissioning of the machines. Like the first stage, this process utilizes the secure EVM Management System developed by the Election Commission. However, the authority conducting this phase shifts to the Returning Officer, who performs the allocation in the direct presence of the candidates or their authorized representatives, as well as independent General Observers appointed to monitor the fairness of the election. The specific goal of this second randomization is to assign the EVMs and VVPATs to individual polling stations within the constituency, thereby breaking any remaining link between a machine's identity and its final destination until the last possible moment. The resulting list from this second randomization, which details exactly which machines are assigned to which polling booths, is then provided directly to the candidates, completing the transparent chain of custody and allocation.

The final and most critical operational phase is the commissioning of the Candidate Set EVMs and VVPATs, which occurs after the last date for the withdrawal of candidatures, ensuring that the final list of contestants is fixed. This commissioning process is carried out for all assembly constituencies or segments in separate, dedicated halls under the strict supervision of the Returning Officer or Assistant Returning Officer. To guarantee that all stakeholders have the opportunity to witness this vital procedure, the Returning Officer issues written intimation to every candidate regarding the schedule, inviting them to be present for oversight and active participation. The venue for this operation is a highly sanitized hall equipped with a single entry and exit point to control access strictly, guarded by at least one section of armed police force around the clock and monitored by twenty-four-seven closed-circuit television coverage to create an immutable record of events. Entry into this secure zone is tightly regulated; permission to pass through the door frame metal detector is granted only upon the production of a valid identity card or an official pass issued by the District Election Officer, ensuring that no unauthorized individuals can enter the premises.

Inside the secure hall, the technical preparation of the Ballot Units begins with the precise installation of the ballot paper, which lists the candidates and their symbols. Officials then set the Thumb Wheel Switch, a mechanical device used to configure the number of candidates, and proceed to mask any unused buttons to prevent accidental presses. The Ballot Screen and the main cabinet are then sealed using address tags and special pink paper seals to indicate any breach of security. Simultaneously, the VVPAT machines are prepared by installing a new thermal paper roll and a fresh power pack. The candidate symbols are loaded into the VVPAT memory using a dedicated Symbol Loading Unit, a process that is projected onto a large television or monitor so that all present can simultaneously view and verify that the correct symbols are being programmed. Once loaded, the paper roll compartment is sealed with address tags. The Control Unit, which acts as the central processor, is prepared by installing a new power pack and configuring the candidate settings by physically connecting it to the prepared Ballot Unit and VVPAT. The Candidate Set section and the Power Pack section of the Control Unit are then sealed with address tags to prevent internal tampering.

To handle any unforeseen technical failures on election day, specific Reserve Units consisting of Ballot Units, Control Units, and VVPATs are identified and prepared. These reserve machines are marked distinctly by pasting adhesive stickers with the word RESERVE on their carrying cases; pink stickers are used for Assembly Elections while white stickers are designated for Parliamentary Elections, allowing for quick visual identification. Before the machines are declared ready for use, a high-rate mock poll is conducted to verify their functionality under realistic conditions. While one hundred percent of VVPATs are checked by casting a single vote for each candidate to ensure the paper printing mechanism is accurate, a more intensive mock poll involving one thousand votes is performed on five percent of the randomly selected EVMs and VVPATs. During this test, the electronic results displayed on the Control Units are rigorously cross-verified with the count of physical VVPAT slips to ensure perfect synchronization between the digital and physical records. Furthermore, to ensure inclusivity, Braille signage features embedded on the Ballot Units are verified, and dummy ballot sheets are prepared to facilitate visually impaired electors in exercising their franchise independently. Finally, to maintain the secrecy of the test votes, all VVPAT paper slips generated during this commissioning phase are destroyed on a daily basis using industrial shredding machines. It is a mandatory requirement that all seals applied during this commissioning process must be signed by the Returning Officers and the candidates or their agents to validate the integrity of the sealing. For comprehensive technical details and procedural nuances, election officials and observers are directed to refer to the latest edition of the EVM Manual and the Returning Officer Handbook, which serve as the definitive guides for the entire electoral machinery operation.

The procedural framework governing the dispersal of Electronic Voting Machines and Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail units is a critical component of election logistics, designed to ensure transparency, security, and trust in the electoral process. The process begins when the Returning Officer, who serves as the chief administrative authority for a specific constituency, issues a formal written invitation to every candidate contesting the election. This invitation specifies the exact date and time for the opening of the secure strong room where the voting machines are stored prior to their dispatch. The purpose of this notification is to allow candidates or their authorized representatives to witness the entire procedure, thereby eliminating any suspicion of tampering before the machines leave the central storage facility. Once the strong room is opened in the presence of these observers, the actual dispersal of the Electronic Voting Machines and their accompanying paper audit trail units commences. This movement of sensitive equipment is executed under strict and proper security arrangements, often involving police escorts and sealed transport vehicles, to prevent unauthorized access or interference during transit to the various polling stations. To facilitate the logistical complexity of moving these machines across large geographical areas, the election commission designates specific locations known as EVM-Intermediate Strong Rooms. These facilities serve as temporary secure holding points where the polling teams, along with the machines, can stay both before the polling day and after the conclusion of voting. The list of these intermediate locations is shared with all contesting candidates well in advance, granting them the right to nominate their own representatives to monitor the safety and integrity of the machines while they are stationed at these intermediate points, ensuring continuous oversight throughout the chain of custody.

Moving beyond the physical machinery, the legal framework also provides alternative voting mechanisms for citizens who cannot physically present themselves at their assigned polling stations on election day. While the standard norm for exercising the franchise requires an elector to vote in person at the specific polling station to which they are assigned based on their residence, the law recognizes that certain categories of voters face genuine hardships or official obligations that prevent physical attendance. Consequently, provisions have been made to allow these specific groups to cast their votes through postal ballot papers. In elections for the Lok Sabha, which is the lower house of the national parliament, and for State Legislative Assemblies, several distinct categories of electors are eligible for this facility. The first category comprises Special Voters, a group consisting of high-ranking constitutional officeholders such as the President, the Vice-President, and the Governors of states. These individuals are declared eligible by the President under Section 20(4) of the Representation of the People Act of 1950 and are registered to vote in their native constituencies despite being stationed elsewhere due to their official duties. The second major category includes Service Voters, which encompasses personnel of the armed forces and paramilitary forces who are registered in their home constituencies, members of the Armed Police Force of a state who are posted outside that particular state, and government employees of India serving in foreign countries. These individuals are registered as electors under Section 20(3) of the Representation of the People Act of 1950.

In addition to special and service voters, the postal ballot facility extends to several other vulnerable or duty-bound groups. This includes voters who are themselves on election duty, meaning they are working as poll officials and therefore cannot vote at their own designated booths. It also covers electors who are under preventive detention, ensuring that their legal restriction does not strip them of their democratic rights. Furthermore, to promote inclusivity, senior citizens who have attained the age of eighty years or above are permitted to vote by post, acknowledging the physical challenges they may face in traveling to polling stations. Similarly, persons with disabilities, specifically those with a benchmark disability of forty percent or more, are granted this concession to ensure accessible participation in the electoral process. The framework also allows for electors serving in identified categories of essential services, as notified by the Election Commission, to utilize postal ballots. Finally, there is a provision for any other class of persons that the Commission, in consultation with the government, may notify as eligible to vote by post. These provisions are grounded in Section 60 of the Representation of the People Act of 1951 and Rule 18 of the Conduct of Elections Rules of 1961, forming a comprehensive legal basis for remote voting.

Within the broader category of service voters, a unique alternative mechanism exists for members of the armed forces and paramilitary forces. Unlike other service voters who must rely solely on postal ballots, these personnel have the option to appoint a proxy voter to cast a vote on their behalf. To be eligible as a proxy, the appointed individual must be at least eighteen years of age, must be an ordinary resident of the constituency in question, and must not be disqualified from being enrolled in the electoral roll. This proxy system offers flexibility, allowing service members to choose between receiving a postal ballot or having a trusted representative vote for them at the polling station assigned to the area covering the service voter's house address. Those service voters who choose to appoint a proxy are formally classified as Classified Service Voters. For these individuals, the proxy travels to the designated polling station to cast the vote physically. However, for service voters who do not opt for proxy voting, the traditional postal ballot remains the primary method, and ballot papers must be sent to all such eligible voters whose names appear in the final part of the electoral roll.

A significant modernization of this process was introduced through an amendment to the Conduct of Elections Rules of 1961, notified on October 21, 2016. This amendment empowered the Returning Officer to transmit postal ballot papers to service voters using electronic means, as specified by the Election Commission of India. Under the provisions of the Second Proviso to sub-rule (1) of Rule 23 of the said rules, the Commission established a specific protocol for this electronic transmission. The Returning Officer is required to electronically transmit a complete set of documents to the service voter. This digital package includes the postal ballot paper itself, Form 13-A which serves as a declaration by the elector, a label for Form 13-B which acts as the inner envelope cover, a label for Form 13-C which serves as the outer envelope cover, and Form 13-D containing detailed instructions for the guidance of the elector. The timeline for this operation is strictly regulated; as soon as the list of contesting candidates is finalized after the withdrawal period concludes, the Returning Officer prepares the postal ballot papers for service voters. By the day immediately following the last date for the withdrawal of candidatures, these documents must be uploaded onto a dedicated platform known as the Electronically Transmitted Postal Ballot Management System.

The operational workflow for a service voter utilizing this electronic system involves a hybrid approach combining digital delivery with physical return. Upon receiving the notification that their ballot materials are available, the service voter accesses the Electronically Transmitted Postal Ballot Management System to download the necessary documents. The voter must then take a physical printout of the ballot paper and the associated forms. The actual voting is cast in physical mode on the printed ballot, adhering to the prescribed manner of marking preferences. Crucially, the voter must sign the declaration of identity on the printed form and have this signature attested by an appropriate authority to verify their identity. Once the vote is cast and the declaration is attested, the voter places the marked ballot into a small envelope and seals it along with the attested declaration inside a larger outer envelope. This sealed package is then dispatched back to the Returning Officer of the concerned constituency via speed post. Thus, the system creates a secure loop where the onward transmission of the blank ballot is achieved rapidly through electronic channels to overcome geographical distances, while the return of the voted ballot follows the conventional and secure postal route to ensure the physical integrity of the cast vote.

For a postal ballot to be validly included in the final vote tally, it is an absolute procedural requirement that the ballot paper must physically reach the office of the Returning Officer prior to the specific time officially designated for the commencement of the counting process. Any ballot arriving after this strict deadline is rendered invalid and cannot be considered during the enumeration of votes. This timing constraint ensures the integrity and synchronization of the election results across all voting methods. Following this fundamental rule, the regulations provide detailed specifications regarding the form and language of postal ballot papers, which are categorized into two distinct types based on the class of the voter. The first category comprises postal ballot papers designed specifically for service voters, who are typically members of the armed forces or other government services deployed away from their home constituencies. The second category encompasses postal ballot papers intended for all other classes of electors who are legally entitled to vote by post but do not fall under the definition of service voters. It is crucial to understand that these two types of ballots differ significantly in their layout, printed information, and visual elements to suit the specific needs and verification processes associated with each voter group.

Regarding the postal ballot papers issued to service voters, the document mandates that all essential particulars, including the names of the contesting candidates and their respective political party affiliations, must be printed in both the official language of the state in which the election is being held and in English. This bilingual requirement ensures that voters from diverse linguistic backgrounds can accurately identify their preferred candidates. Furthermore, to aid in positive identification, a photograph of each candidate must be printed directly adjacent to their name.
