The use or operation of any apparatus designed to amplify or reproduce the human voice, such as megaphones or loudspeakers, is prohibited during the silence period within the polling station, at its entrance, or in its immediate neighborhood. Furthermore, shouting or behaving in a disorderly manner within these zones is also classified as a violation. It is crucial to note that the physical distance from which a loudspeaker or similar device is operated is considered immaterial in determining an offense; even if the device is used from a location more than one hundred meters away, an offense is still committed if the noise causes annoyance to any person visiting the polling station or interferes with the duties of the officers and staff working at the station.

Special attention is drawn to Section 134B of the Representation of the People Act of 1951, which underlines the legislative intent to ensure that, except for those expressly permitted by the statute, no individual carries weapons or engages in any display of arms within polling stations or their vicinity. Consequently, the Election Commission has issued clear instructions stating that no person, regardless of whether they are a recipient of security protection from any agency, should enter a polling station or approach its neighborhood accompanied by their usual security detail. However, a specific exception exists for candidates who are protected by the Special Protection Group, or SPG. In such cases, the Close Protection Team, armed with weapons, may accompany the protectee only up to the door of the polling booth. Once inside, only one Personal Security Officer carrying a concealed firearm is permitted to accompany the candidate. This officer must position themselves strategically to provide necessary cover to the protectee should an emergency arise, but they must do so without interfering in any manner with the electoral proceedings or influencing the voters.

Regarding campaign infrastructure, instructions issued by the Commission dictate that no election booths established by candidates are allowed to be set up in the immediate vicinity of the polling station on the day of the poll. The presence of such booths is deemed detrimental as they create obstructions for voters, potentially lead to confrontations among workers of different political parties, and generate law and order challenges that could disrupt the smooth conduct of free and fair elections. Despite this restriction, candidates are permitted to provide a minimal setup consisting of one table and two chairs for the use of their agents and workers. This setup is intended solely for the distribution of unofficial identity slips to voters and must be located beyond a distance of two hundred meters from the polling station. To ensure the comfort of the workers, an umbrella or a piece of tarpaulin may be placed over the table to provide protection from sun or rain, but strict measures must be enforced to ensure that no crowd is allowed to gather around these tables, preventing them from becoming de facto campaign hubs. Finally, the legal framework treats the prevention of voting as a severe crime. It is classified as a cognizable offense to detain any voter in a camp or any other location, whether with or without the voter's consent, to obstruct their path to the polling station, or to prevent them from voting in any manner whatsoever. This provision empowers law enforcement to act immediately without a warrant to protect the fundamental right of citizens to participate in the democratic process.

In the event that a candidate becomes aware of any individual being unlawfully detained, obstructed, or prevented from reaching a polling station to cast their vote, there is a clear and immediate duty to report this incident. The candidate must notify the Presiding Officer in charge of the polling station, contact the nearest police station, or inform the Returning Officer who oversees the election in that constituency. These authorities are empowered and obligated to take swift and effective action to ensure that any person who is wrongly held back or hindered is able to exercise their fundamental right to vote, known as their franchise. This protection extends even to situations where the detention or obstruction is occurring within a private property or residence, emphasizing that no individual or group has the authority to suppress voting rights regardless of the location. The legal framework is designed to remove all barriers to participation, ensuring that the democratic process remains inclusive and free from coercion or physical prevention.

A critical aspect of maintaining electoral integrity involves the strict prohibition against the illegal hiring of vehicles for the conveyance of voters on polling day. It is explicitly mandated that a candidate must not allow any person acting on their behalf to provide transportation for voters to or from the polling booth. Engaging in such activity is classified as both a corrupt practice and a specific electoral offence under the law. If a candidate discovers that another candidate, or any of that candidate's agents, supporters, or workers, is providing such free conveyance, they are required to lodge a formal complaint immediately at the scene. Taking action at that precise moment is crucial because it allows for the collection of vital evidence that might otherwise be lost if the complaint is merely sent later to election authorities. This immediate reporting enables the prosecution of the offender under section 133 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, which is the primary legislation governing the conduct of elections in India. Furthermore, failing to act promptly can weaken the case against the violators. The candidate may also utilize this violation as grounds for filing an election petition after the results are declared, as the provision of free transport to voters is a serious corrupt practice that can invalidate an election outcome. Additionally, the candidate's polling agent, who is an official observer stationed at the booth, has the authority to submit a written complaint to the Presiding Officer. The Presiding Officer will then forward this document to the relevant Magistrate who has the legal jurisdiction to handle such cases. The candidate may also request that the Presiding Officer record their own observations and remarks on the complaint, adding an official layer of verification to the allegation.

To effectively check the corrupt practice and electoral offence of providing free conveyance for voters, the Election Commission has devised a comprehensive scheme for the regulation of traffic on the day of the poll. The primary objective of this scheme is to cordon off polling areas from all unnecessary vehicular traffic to prevent voters from being transported to and from polling booths in violation of election laws. Under these guidelines, public transport vehicles such as buses and minibuses are generally permitted to operate on their regular routes. However, strict vigilance must be maintained to ensure that these public vehicles are not used clandestinely or secretly to pick up and drop off voters specifically for the purpose of influencing their vote. Private cars and taxies are also allowed on the roads, but only if they are carrying passengers to legitimate destinations other than polling booths, such as hospitals, airports, railway stations, bus stands, private residences of friends and relatives, clubs, or restaurants. Despite this allowance, these private vehicles are strictly prohibited from approaching polling areas surreptitiously to convey voters. Traffic management plans must be implemented to divert these vehicles away from the immediate vicinity of polling stations to effectively control and prevent their misuse for electoral purposes. Similarly, the movement of heavy goods vehicles like lorries and trucks will be regulated in the same manner to avoid any loopholes. The District Magistrate and Regional Transport Authorities are instructed to enforce these regulations fully, adhering to both the letter and the spirit of the law, and a formal certificate confirming this enforcement must be obtained from the concerned authorities. Political parties and contesting candidates, along with their agents and workers, must be formally warned that any contravention of these traffic regulations will receive serious attention. Such violations can provide sufficient material to challenge the validity of an election through an election petition and may also invite penal action under criminal law.

Specific allowances regarding vehicle usage are granted to contesting candidates to facilitate their official election duties while maintaining fairness. For an election to the House of the People, which refers to the lower house of the Indian Parliament, each contesting candidate is entitled to a limited number of authorized vehicles. Specifically, a candidate is allowed one vehicle for their own personal use across the entire constituency. In addition, they are permitted one vehicle for the exclusive use of their election agent throughout the constituency. Furthermore, the candidate is entitled to one additional vehicle for the use of their workers or party workers in each of the assembly segments that comprise the larger Parliamentary Constituency. This structure ensures that campaign teams have necessary mobility without flooding the area with unregulated transport. For elections to the State Legislative Assembly, the entitlements are slightly adjusted but follow a similar logic. Each contesting candidate is entitled to one vehicle for their own use, one vehicle for their election agent, and one additional vehicle for their workers or party workers. A crucial stipulation attached to these permissions is that if the candidate is absent from the constituency on polling day, no other person is allowed to use the vehicle that was specifically allotted for the candidate's personal use. This prevents the misuse of candidate-specific permits when the candidate is not present to oversee operations.

To ensure transparency and easy identification of authorized vehicles, strict formatting requirements are imposed on the permits issued. The permits must be printed with bold letters displaying the legend "Election Permit." Furthermore, these documents must bear the super inscription of the official seal and the signature of the authority issuing the permit. These permits must be displayed prominently on the windscreen of the vehicle so that law enforcement and election officials can instantly verify their authenticity. Candidates are expected to follow the guidelines of the Commission's scheme meticulously. It is the responsibility of the candidate to obtain these permits for all vehicles to be used by themselves and their party workers well in advance of polling day. Failure to secure proper documentation or adherence to the routing and usage restrictions can lead to severe consequences, including the impounding of vehicles and legal action against the candidate. Finally, the legal framework strictly defines the removal of ballot papers or Electronic Voting Machines, commonly known as EVMs, from a polling station as a grave offence. Ballot papers are the physical records of votes cast, while EVMs are the electronic devices used to record votes digitally. The unauthorized removal, tampering, or transport of these critical election materials out of the secured polling station environment undermines the entire electoral process and is treated with the utmost severity by law to protect the sanctity of the vote count.

In the event that a contesting candidate or any of their authorized representatives holds an honest and reasonable suspicion that a voter is engaging in unlawful conduct, such as illegally removing a ballot paper or a voting machine from the premises of a polling station, or if there is belief that such equipment is being handled in an unauthorized manner or subjected to fraudulent tampering, it is the immediate duty of that candidate or agent to bring this matter to the direct attention of the Presiding Officer. This requirement ensures that any potential breach of electoral integrity is addressed instantly by the official in charge, who is empowered to take necessary corrective actions to safeguard the sanctity of the voting process. This protocol serves as a critical checkpoint against malpractice, relying on the vigilance of party representatives to act as watchdogs alongside the official election machinery.

Regarding the specific role and deployment of polling agents, every candidate who is contesting an election possesses the statutory right to appoint one primary polling agent and two relief agents to serve at each individual polling station within their constituency. However, strict limitations are placed on their physical presence to maintain order and prevent overcrowding inside the sensitive area of the polling booth. At any given moment, only one of these appointed individuals is permitted to remain inside the polling station. It is mandatory to ensure that whether it is the main polling agent or a substitute relief agent, no more than a single representative occupies the interior space simultaneously. The Presiding Officer, who acts as the head of the polling station, is responsible for briefing these agents upon their arrival. This briefing includes instructions that they must remain present throughout the entire duration of the voting process until it concludes. Their presence is particularly crucial during the final stages when the Electronic Voting Machines, commonly known as EVMs, and the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail systems, referred to as VVPATs, are sealed. The agents are required to oversee this sealing procedure to ensure transparency and must subsequently sign various declaration forms to certify that the process was conducted correctly. To maintain a clear record of attendance and movement, a specific movement sheet is provided at every polling station. Every polling agent and relief agent is required to enter their details on this sheet, specifically signing to indicate the exact time of their arrival at the station and the precise time of their departure. This documentation creates an auditable trail of who was present and when. Furthermore, the Election Commission has issued specific directives regarding the eligibility of certain high-profile individuals to serve as polling agents. Ministers serving in the Union government or State governments, as well as Members of Parliament, Members of Legislative Assemblies, and Members of Legislative Councils, are prohibited from being appointed as polling agents. This restriction also extends to any other person who has been provided with security cover by the State. The rationale behind this rule is practical and safety-oriented; the security personnel assigned to protect these VIPs cannot be permitted to enter the polling station, yet the safety of the VIP cannot be jeopardized by requiring them to enter without their protective detail. Consequently, no individual enjoying such security cover is allowed to surrender or waive their security protection merely to qualify for appointment as a polling agent.

The formal appointment of these polling agents must be executed in writing using a standardized document known as Form 10, which is detailed in Annexure 13 of the election manuals. There are specific residency and voter registration requirements for those appointed to this role. Ideally, polling agents appointed by a candidate should be ordinary residents and registered voters within the same polling station where they are deployed. This ensures that the agents have a direct stake and familiarity with the local electorate. However, recognizing that a contesting candidate might face difficulties in finding a suitable agent within the immediate polling station or even in a neighboring polling station, provisions exist to broaden the pool of eligible candidates. In such cases, the candidate is permitted to appoint any registered elector from the same assembly constituency to serve as their polling agent for that specific station. A fundamental requirement for all such agents is the possession of a valid Electors Photo Identity Card, which serves as proof of their identity and voting status. If a person is a registered elector in the relevant polling area and is proposed for appointment but currently lacks this photo identity card, the candidate or their election agent may submit a written request to the Returning Officer. The Returning Officer is then obligated to make the necessary arrangements to issue an Electors Photo Identity Card to that individual promptly. Once appointed, all polling agents are mandated to display their Electors Photo Identity Cards prominently on their person while on duty. Interestingly, the candidate is not required to send a copy of the appointment order to the Returning Officer beforehand. Instead, the polling agent must carry the original appointment order to the polling station and produce it before the Presiding Officer, who will verify the document before admitting the agent into the facility. As part of the appointment process, the polling agent must sign the letter of appointment in Form 10, which acts as a token of their agreement to act on behalf of the candidate. It is highly advisable that this signature be affixed in the physical presence of the candidate or, if the appointment is made by the election agent, in the presence of that agent. Upon arriving at the polling station, the agent will be required to sign again, this time in the presence of the Presiding Officer. The officer should instruct the agent to sign in the exact same manner as they did before the candidate or election agent. This consistency is vital to ensure there is no discrepancy between the signatures, thereby facilitating the ready acceptance of the agent identity and preventing disputes over authentication.

To facilitate the verification process described above, the Returning Officer plays a pivotal administrative role prior to election day. To enable the Presiding Officer of any polling station to accurately verify the signature of the candidate or their election agent as it appears on the appointment letter in Form 10, the Returning Officer must first obtain the specimen signature of the candidate or the election agent. This is done using a specific format found in Annexure 15. Once collected, sufficient copies of this format containing the specimen signatures are distributed to every Presiding Officer, as well as to the Observers appointed by the Commission, Zonal Magistrates, Sector Magistrates, and other relevant officials operating within the constituency. This distribution ensures that every official point of contact has the reference material needed to validate appointments on the spot. In scenarios where a candidate has chosen not to appoint an election agent, the column designated for the election agent signature on the aforementioned format must explicitly record the words No Election Agent Appointed to avoid confusion. The rules also provide flexibility regarding the tenure of these appointments. A candidate retains the liberty to revoke the appointment of an election agent at any time and may appoint another person to fill that position. This ability to change representation ensures that candidates can adapt their team strategy as needed while maintaining the integrity of the appointment verification system through the centralized collection of specimen signatures.

In the intricate administrative framework governing electoral processes, specific protocols are established to manage changes in the appointment of election personnel, particularly when such changes occur at a critical juncture immediately preceding the commencement of voting. If a modification regarding the appointment of an election agent is effected at the very last moment, specifically after the Presiding Officer has already been provided with the official document known as Format Annexure fifteen which contains the specimen signatures originally submitted by the candidate, the burden of rectification falls squarely upon the candidate. In this scenario, it becomes the mandatory responsibility of the candidate to furnish the Presiding Officer with two distinct documents to ensure the legal validity of the representation. The first document is Form nine, which serves as a formal instrument of revocation to legally terminate the appointment of the previously designated election agent. The second document is Form eight, which acts as the official letter of appointment for the newly selected election agent. This dual submission ensures that the records held by the Presiding Officer accurately reflect the current authorized representation, thereby preventing any confusion or dispute regarding who is legally empowered to act on behalf of the candidate during the polling process.

Beyond the role of the election agent, the regulations also provide a structured mechanism for the management of polling agents, who are individuals appointed to observe the voting process at specific polling stations on behalf of a candidate. Either the candidate personally or their duly appointed election agent holds the authority to revoke the appointment of any polling agent through a written declaration using Form eleven, which is cataloged as Annexure fourteen. This written order of revocation must be formally lodged with the Presiding Officer of the relevant polling station to take effect. Furthermore, the rules account for unforeseen circumstances such as the death of a polling agent prior to the poll; in such tragic events, the candidate or their election agent is obligated to promptly inform the Presiding Officer of the demise. Whether the vacancy arises due to a voluntary revocation or the death of the agent, the candidate or their election agent retains the right to appoint a replacement. This is achieved by issuing a fresh letter of appointment utilizing Form ten, following the same procedural standards as the initial appointment. However, to maintain transparency and an accurate audit trail, this new letter of appointment must explicitly state the factual circumstances that necessitated the new appointment, such as specifying whether the previous agent was revoked or had passed away.

The timing of the arrival of polling agents at the polling station is governed by strict guidelines designed to ensure the integrity and transparency of the pre-poll procedures. A polling agent is required to arrive at the polling station no later than ninety minutes before the scheduled commencement of the poll. This early arrival window is not arbitrary but is calculated to ensure that the agent is present during critical preparatory activities conducted by the Presiding Officer. These activities include the physical preparation and setting up of the Electronic Voting Machines, commonly referred to as EVMs, and the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail units, known as VVPATs, which provide a physical record of votes cast. The presence of the agent during these preliminaries allows them to witness the sealing of machines and other logistical arrangements, thereby building confidence in the fairness of the process. It is crucial to note that the electoral timeline is rigid; if any part of these preliminary procedures has already been completed before a late-arriving agent appears, the proceedings will not be restarted or commenced de-novo simply to accommodate the tardy individual. To facilitate necessary movement, each polling agent is issued a pass by the Presiding Officer, which serves as the official authorization allowing the agent to enter and exit the polling station as required by their duties without hindrance.

Candidates are strongly advised to exercise diligence in ensuring their polling agents reach the assigned stations well in advance of the poll's start time. This proactive measure is essential to guarantee that all pre-poll formalities, including the issuance of entry passes and the conduct of the mock poll, are executed smoothly and within the stipulated timeframe. The mock poll is a vital dress rehearsal conducted to verify that the voting machines are functioning correctly and recording votes as intended. The Presiding Officer is mandated to conduct this mock poll strictly in the presence of the polling agents. Following the successful completion of this test, the Presiding Officer must prepare and sign a mock poll certificate using the prescribed format identified as Annexure seventeen. As part of this certification process, the Presiding Officer is required to record the names of the attending agents and the candidates they represent, subsequently obtaining the signatures of these agents on the certificate. This step creates a binding record that the agents witnessed the proper functioning of the equipment before actual voting began.

The scheduling of the mock poll is precisely defined to occur normally ninety minutes before the scheduled hour for the commencement of the poll. To ensure adequate attendance, all contesting candidates must be informed in writing well in advance that the mock poll will begin at this specific time, and they should be instructed to direct their polling agents to be present punctually. The regulations stipulate that the mock poll should ideally proceed with the polling agents of at least two different candidates present to serve as independent witnesses. However, the electoral machinery is designed to prevent delays caused by non-appearance. If the agents of at least two candidates are not present at the scheduled time, the Presiding Officer is authorized to wait for an additional fifteen minutes. If, after this fifteen-minute grace period, the requisite number of agents still has not arrived, the Presiding Officer is empowered to proceed with the mock poll regardless of the number of agents present. It is explicitly clarified that even if only one agent or no agents at all are present after the waiting period, the Presiding Officer must go ahead and start the mock poll to avoid delaying the entire election process. In such instances where the mock poll is conducted with fewer than the ideal number of witnesses, the mock poll certificate must contain a specific mention detailing this aspect, thereby documenting the deviation from the standard witnessing requirement for future reference and accountability.

To maintain overarching supervision and rapid response capabilities, the Returning Officer, who oversees the election in a broader constituency, utilizes a hierarchy of Sector Officers and established communication links with individual polling stations and mobile teams to track the conduct and status of the mock poll. The objective is to ascertain the completion status of the mock poll across all stations within thirty minutes of the scheduled time. A failure to receive confirmation that a mock poll has been conducted is treated as a significant red flag, potentially indicative of technical malfunctions with the Electronic Voting Machines or procedural lapses on the part of the Presiding Officer. Consequently, the Returning Officer is required to make immediate interventions to diagnose and resolve any such problems swiftly. Furthermore, polling stations where rival candidates from recognized political parties are absent, necessitating the conduct of the mock poll without their agents, are flagged for focused attention by the election machinery. In these sensitive scenarios, enhanced security and monitoring measures are deployed, which may include the assignment of reserve micro-observers, the installation of video cameras to record proceedings, and frequent visits by Sector Officers and other senior officials to ensure that the absence of party agents does not compromise the integrity of the electoral process.

Following the conclusion of the mock poll, a critical preliminary step in the electoral process designed to verify the functionality of voting equipment before actual voting begins, the Returning Officer must undertake a specific and rigorous administrative procedure based on the certifications submitted by Presiding Officers. The Returning Officer is mandated to compile a comprehensive list identifying those specific polling stations where the mock poll was conducted under compromised conditions, specifically instances where no agents were present to witness the procedure or situations where agents representing more than one rival candidate from recognized political parties were absent during the test. This scrutiny is essential because the presence of multiple competing agents ensures transparency and mutual verification; their absence creates a potential vulnerability that requires heightened oversight. Consequently, special and focused attention must be directed toward all documents and records originating from these identified polling stations to ensure proper scrutiny and validate the integrity of the process. Election observers, who are independent officials appointed to monitor the fairness of the election, are also explicitly instructed to pay close attention to this particular aspect of the post-mock poll review to guarantee that any irregularities are detected and addressed before the main polling commences.

The role of the polling agent is fundamental to the safeguarding of democratic principles at the grassroots level, with their primary duty being the protection of the interests of the specific candidate who has appointed them at that particular polling station. Beyond this overarching responsibility, the polling agent has a detailed set of operational duties that serve as checks and balances within the polling environment. One of their key functions is to assist the Presiding Officer in detecting and preventing voter impersonation, a serious electoral offense where an individual attempts to vote using the identity of another registered voter; the agent achieves this by formally challenging individuals whose identity or eligibility they suspect to be fraudulent. Furthermore, the agent plays a crucial role in the physical security of the electronic voting infrastructure, helping to ensure that the Voting Machines are properly secured and sealed in strict accordance with established rules both before the polling starts and after it concludes, thereby maintaining the chain of custody. Upon the close of the poll, the agent is entitled to receive and must collect a copy of the account of votes recorded along with the paper seal account, documented in a specific official document known as Form 17-C, which serves as an immediate record of the vote count at that station. The agent must also verify that all other documents relating to the conduct of the poll are secured and sealed properly as required by law to prevent tampering. Additionally, the agent is required to actively participate in the mock poll to personally satisfy themselves that the Electronic Voting Machines, commonly referred to as EVMs, and the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail units, known as VVPATs, are in proper working order and functioning correctly. Finally, the agent must cross-verify that the serial numbers of the Control Units, which manage the voting process, the Ballot Units, where votes are cast, and the VVPATs, which generate paper slips for verification, match exactly with the details previously provided by the Returning Officer to ensure no unauthorized equipment has been substituted.

To support these agents in fulfilling their complex responsibilities, the Election Commission has published a dedicated resource titled the Handbook for Polling Agents. This comprehensive guide contains detailed instructions, legal precedents, and procedural guidelines necessary for effective monitoring. Candidates are encouraged to download sufficient copies of this handbook from the official website of the Commission and distribute them among their appointed polling agents if they deem it necessary, ensuring that every agent is fully informed of their rights and duties. In preparation for their deployment, a polling agent must equip themselves with specific mandatory items before arriving at the polling station. These essentials include the original letter of appointment issued in the standardized Format 10, which serves as their official credential and proof of authorization. They must also carry a copy of the latest integrated electoral roll specific to that polling station, allowing them to verify voter lists against actual attendees. While optional, it is advisable for an agent to bring a small brass seal if they intend to place their own unique seal on the carrying cases of the Ballot Units, Control Units, and VVPATs before these machines are transported to the Counting or Receipt Centre, adding an extra layer of security. Basic stationery such as pens, paper, and pencils are also required for taking notes and recording observations, alongside the detailed specifications of the EVM components and VVPATs to be used at the station, as provided by either the Returning Officer or the candidate.

Strict regulations govern the appearance and conduct of individuals within the polling environment to maintain neutrality and prevent any form of campaigning on the day of the election. No person is permitted to enter the polling station or the area within one hundred meters of its boundaries while wearing badges, emblems, or any attire bearing the names of candidates, political leaders, or their associated symbols and pictorial representations, as such displays could be interpreted as active campaigning for a contesting candidate, which is legally prohibited during this period. This prohibition extends specifically to wearable items such as caps, shawls, or scarves that feature a political party's name, symbol, or slogan, none of which are allowed inside the polling station on poll day. However, an exception is made strictly for identification purposes; polling agents are permitted to display a badge on their person that shows only the name of the candidate they represent. This limited display is authorized solely to facilitate their immediate identification by election officials and other stakeholders, without constituting promotional material.

Access to the polling station is highly restricted to ensure order and secrecy, with the Presiding Officer authorized to admit only a specific category of individuals besides the registered electors. Those granted entry include the polling officers who manage the station, each candidate in person, their election agent, and one polling agent representing each candidate at any given time to avoid overcrowding. Entry is also permitted for persons specifically authorized by the Election Commission, public servants who are on duty in connection with the election, and observers appointed by the Commission to monitor proceedings. It is important to clarify that the term public servants on election duty does not automatically include police officers, unless the Presiding Officer has specifically called for their assistance to maintain law and order or for another explicit purpose within the station. Furthermore, high-ranking government officials such as Ministers, Ministers of State, and Deputy Ministers of the Union or State governments are excluded from this category when acting in their official capacity. Additional exceptions allow for a child in arms accompanying an elector, a person assisting a blind or infirm voter who cannot move without help, and such other persons as the Presiding Officer may occasionally admit to assist in identifying voters or facilitating the poll. A critical security provision dictates that no security personnel accompanying an elector, candidate, election agent, or polling agent shall be allowed to enter the one-hundred-meter perimeter known as the Polling Station Neighbourhood, nor are they permitted inside the polling booth itself, ensuring that the immediate voting area remains free from armed influence. Finally, to ensure the comfort and effectiveness of the monitoring process, the Presiding Officer is responsible for making suitable seating arrangements for polling agents when they attend the poll, allowing them to observe proceedings clearly without obstructing the flow of voters.

The physical arrangement of seating within a polling station is a critical component of electoral integrity, designed to balance the need for transparency with the absolute requirement of voter secrecy. Polling agents, who represent the interests of various candidates, must be seated in locations that provide them with an adequate and unobstructed opportunity to observe the entire electoral process. This includes having a clear line of sight to the table managed by the Presiding Officer or the third Polling Officer, which is the designated location for the Control Unit, the electronic device that manages the voting data. Furthermore, these agents must be able to visually track the movement of every voter as they proceed from the officer's table to the private voting compartment and subsequently exit the compartment after recording their vote on the Ballot Unit housed inside. However, this observational privilege has a strict and non-negotiable boundary. Under no circumstances should polling agents be seated in a position that allows them to see the specific moment a voter records their choice within the privacy of the voting compartment. Such visibility would fundamentally compromise the secrecy of the ballot, a cornerstone of democratic freedom, and therefore the seating layout must be meticulously planned to prevent any accidental or intentional viewing of the voting act itself. To ensure fairness in how these observation spots are allocated, the Election Commission has established a hierarchical system of priorities for seating polling agents. This hierarchy begins with the highest priority given to candidates representing recognized national parties, followed by candidates from recognized state parties. The third tier of priority is assigned to candidates from recognized state parties who have received special permission to use their reserved symbols within that specific constituency. Following them are candidates from registered but unrecognized political parties, and finally, independent candidates who are not affiliated with any registered party receive the last priority in seating arrangements. This structured approach ensures that while all candidates have representation, those with broader electoral mandates are afforded primary observation rights.

Before the actual voting commences on election day, a rigorous procedure known as the Mock Poll is conducted at every polling station to verify the accuracy and functionality of the Electronic Voting Machines and the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail systems. The timing of this mock poll is contingent upon the presence of polling agents. If two or more polling agents are present at the station, the mock poll must begin ninety minutes prior to the scheduled start time of the actual election. In scenarios where only one polling agent or no polling agents are available, the waiting period before initiating the mock poll is reduced to fifteen minutes. The physical setup for this test involves placing the Ballot Units and the VVPAT machines inside the voting compartment, while the Control Unit is positioned on the table of the Presiding Officer or the designated Polling Officer in charge of the Control Unit. The connectivity of these devices follows a specific chain; the cable from the VVPAT is connected directly to the Control Unit, and the cable from the Ballot Unit is connected to the VVPAT. In polling stations where multiple Ballot Units are required to accommodate a large number of candidates, these units are cascaded in a series. This means the cable of the first Ballot Unit connects to the VVPAT, the cable of the second Ballot Unit connects to the first Ballot Unit, the third connects to the second, and so on, creating a daisy-chain configuration that allows all units to function under a single Control Unit. Before the system is powered on, the drop box of the VVPAT, which collects the printed paper slips, is shown to the polling agents to visually confirm that it is empty and contains no pre-existing slips, thereby ensuring the count starts from zero. Additionally, the paper roll knob on the VVPAT is unlocked and set to the working position before the Control Unit is switched on. Once powered, the CLEAR button on the Control Unit is pressed to display a zero count, confirming that no votes have been recorded in the memory.

During the mock poll exercise, a minimum of fifty votes must be cast to thoroughly test the system. It is mandatory that the number of votes allocated to each candidate, including the option for None of the Above, is carefully recorded manually to serve as a benchmark for verification. Polling agents are explicitly permitted to participate in casting these mock votes to witness the process firsthand. Upon completion of the fifty votes, the CLOSE button on the Control Unit is pressed to finalize the input phase. Subsequently, the RESULT button is activated to display the tally stored in the machine, which is then compared against the manual record kept by the officials to ensure an exact match. Parallel to this electronic verification, the paper slips generated by the VVPAT during the mock poll are removed from the drop box and counted. These physical slips are tallied against the result displayed on the Control Unit to confirm that the electronic count matches the paper audit trail perfectly. Once the verification is successful, the CLEAR button on the Control Unit is pressed again to erase the mock poll data from the system's memory, resetting the machine for the actual election. The physical paper slips generated during this test are then stamped with an official seal reading MOCK POLL SLIP to distinguish them from valid ballots. These stamped slips are placed into a black envelope, which is then securely sealed using a Pink Paper Seal to prevent tampering. A formal Mock Poll Certificate is completed by the Presiding Officer, and signatures are obtained from both the polling agents and the polling officers to certify that the test was conducted successfully and the results were accurate. Following this certification, the Presiding Officer switches off the Control Unit to facilitate the sealing of the Result Section of the device with a Green Paper Seal, along with a Special Tag and an Address Tag. The drop box of the VVPAT is also secured with an Address Tag. The Presiding Officer signs these seals, and polling agents are invited to sign them as well, providing a multi-party verification of the security measures. Just before the official start time of the actual poll, the Control Unit is switched on once more. Before the first eligible voter signs the Register of Voters, known as Form 17A, the Polling Officer-I must consult with the Presiding Officer and record in ink on the form that the total count in the Control Unit has been checked and found to be zero, establishing a clean baseline for the day's voting.

To assist voters who may be unfamiliar with the technology or require guidance, a specific educational tool is provided to the Presiding Officer. A printed sample of a dummy Electronic Voting Machine Ballot Paper, pasted onto a cardboard cutout that matches the real size of the actual interface, is supplied to all Presiding Officers along with other polling materials at the time of dispatch. This prop serves a vital instructional purpose. Whenever a voter approaches the booth asking for assistance or expresses an inability to operate the Electronic Voting Machine, the Presiding Officer uses this cardboard model to explain the voting process. This explanation is conducted in the presence of polling agents to maintain transparency, ensuring that the voter understands how to select their preferred candidate without the officer ever touching the actual machine or influencing the voter's choice. This method ensures that every citizen, regardless of their technical literacy, can exercise their franchise confidently and correctly.

In the event of equipment malfunction on polling day, a strict replacement protocol is enforced to minimize disruption and maintain the integrity of the vote. The procedure differs depending on whether the issue arises during the pre-poll mock testing phase or during the actual voting hours. If a malfunction is detected during the Mock Poll before the actual election begins, the response is targeted and specific. If only the Ballot Unit fails to function, only that specific unit is replaced. Similarly, if the Control Unit is defective, only the Control Unit is swapped out, and if the VVPAT machine is not working, only the VVPAT is replaced. This modular approach saves time when the system has not yet recorded any genuine voter data. However, once the Actual Poll has commenced, the protocol becomes more stringent to ensure the seamless continuity of the audit trail. If either the Ballot Unit or the Control Unit malfunctions during active voting, the entire set comprising the Ballot Unit, Control Unit, and VVPAT must be replaced as a single integrated unit. In such a scenario, a fresh mock poll is immediately conducted on the new set of machines. This emergency mock poll involves casting exactly one vote for each contesting candidate, including the None of the Above option, followed by the standard verification and sealing procedures to certify the new equipment before resuming public voting. Conversely, if only the VVPAT machine malfunctions during the actual poll while the electronic voting components remain functional, only the VVPAT unit is replaced, allowing the electronic recording of votes to continue with minimal interruption while restoring the paper audit capability.

In the specific scenario where a technical fault necessitates the replacement of a power pack within the Control Unit or the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail system, no mock poll is conducted following the repair. A mock poll is typically a test run performed before actual voting begins to ensure the machine records votes correctly, but in this instance, because the issue is isolated strictly to the battery or power supply component rather than the vote recording logic itself, the testing phase is waived to save time and maintain the polling schedule. If the Control Unit displays an error message explicitly instructing staff to replace the power pack of the Control Unit or the power pack of the VVPAT device, the prescribed procedure is to swap out only the defective power source for that specific unit. It is a mandatory safety and procedural requirement that before any such replacement activity commences, the Control Unit must be completely switched off to prevent electrical damage or data corruption during the hardware exchange.

Handling contingencies that arise during the active polling process requires strict adherence to protocol to ensure the integrity of the vote count. One critical situation involves the VVPAT printer failing to cut the paper slip after printing or failing to print the slip entirely. In such an event, if the paper slip remains attached to the roll and hangs down, election officials are strictly prohibited from attempting to manually remove, tear, or cut the hanging slip, and no effort should be made to force it into the drop box where valid slips are collected. This hanging status is a deliberate mechanical indicator signifying that the vote was not successfully recorded in the memory of the Control Unit. Since the vote was not registered digitally, the uncut slip holds no validity and will not be counted during the final tabulation of paper slips. However, transparency demands that every detail of such an occurrence be meticulously documented in the official diary maintained by the Presiding Officer. This record must include the exact date and time the incident occurred, the full name of the voter involved, and their specific serial number as listed in the electoral roll part. The log must also clarify whether the voter was permitted to cast their ballot again after the VVPAT unit was replaced or if the voter chose to leave without casting a vote due to the disruption. Furthermore, the total number of votes successfully cast prior to this malfunction must be noted, along with confirmation that the last voter was allowed to vote only after the replacement unit was fully operational.

Another potential contingency involves a complaint regarding wrong printing on the VVPAT slip. If a voter alleges that the paper slip printed by the machine displays incorrect particulars of a candidate or the wrong symbol after the voter pressed the blue button corresponding to their chosen candidate on the Balloting Unit, the Presiding Officer at the polling station must immediately provide the voter with a specific document known as the Form of Declaration to formally lodge this complaint. Upon receipt of this form, the officer must follow the detailed legal procedure outlined under rule 49MA of the election conduct rules. As part of this verification process, the complainant is required to cast a mock vote in the presence of both the Presiding Officer and the Polling Agent to demonstrate the alleged discrepancy, ensuring that the complaint is genuine and not a misunderstanding of the interface.

The closure of the poll is governed by rigid timing rules to ensure fairness across all constituencies. The polling process must conclude exactly at the time fixed by the Election Commission, even if the commencement of voting was delayed earlier in the day due to unavoidable reasons such as equipment setup delays or logistical issues. The end time is absolute and cannot be extended to compensate for a late start. Once the last eligible voter in the queue has cast their ballot, the Presiding Officer must press the designated CLOSE button on the Control Unit. This action permanently locks the machine from accepting any further votes. Immediately after closing the poll, the officer must record the total number of votes recorded in the Electronic Voting Machine in Item 6 of Part I of Form 17C, which is a statutory account of the vote count. This same figure must also be entered into the Presiding Officer's Diary for cross-verification. To ensure transparency, a copy of the completed Form 17C is handed over to the Polling Agents representing the various candidates present at the station.

Following the completion of all prescribed forms and the accurate filling of election documents, the Control Unit is switched off to cease all electronic operations. The physical cables connecting the Electronic Voting Machine and the VVPAT unit are then carefully disconnected. These components are placed back into their respective carrying cases, which are then secured using official Address Tags that identify the specific polling station and constituency. In scenarios where simultaneous elections are being held for different bodies, such as parliament and state assemblies, the paperwork and sealing processes for each set of machines must be prepared and sealed separately to avoid any mixing of data or materials. The Presiding Officer is required to sign these address tags personally and must also obtain the signatures of the Polling Agents on the same tags, serving as a joint certification that the seals are intact and the chain of custody is unbroken. After the polling concludes, the polled EVMs and VVPATs, along with all other election materials, are escorted under security to a designated Collection or Reception Centre. Polling Agents are legally entitled to follow the vehicles transporting these materials from the polling station all the way to the collection center to witness the secure transfer firsthand.

On the day of the poll, rigorous protocols govern the movement, storage, and security of both active and reserve voting machines. Reserve EVMs and VVPAT units are pre-positioned and provided to Sector Officers or Zonal Magistrates to handle any immediate failures at polling stations. In areas classified with high sensitivity levels, denoted as P-1, P-2, or P-3, these reserve machines are stored in an intermediate Strong Room, which is a highly secured temporary vault, and advance intimation regarding their location is provided to all contesting candidates. If the reserve machines are issued to officers before the actual polling day, they must be kept in a Government Building equipped with proper security measures until they are needed. Throughout their deployment, whether being transported or stationed, all EVMs and VVPATs assigned to polling parties or sector officers remain under the constant cover of armed police personnel to prevent tampering or theft. The transportation vehicles used for moving these sensitive devices are equipped with GPS tracking systems or mobile application-based tracking to allow real-time monitoring of their location and route.

Specific labeling and handling procedures apply to reserve and non-functional units. Reserve machines and those that were found non-functional before any actual votes were cast are carried by the Sector Officer and must bear clear labels such as RESERVE or MOCK POLL REPLACED to distinguish them from machines that have recorded actual voter data. Any non-functional unit that was replaced before the start of the actual poll, specifically during the mock poll phase, must not be left behind at the polling station with the Presiding Officer; instead, it must be physically carried away by the Sector Officer to a secure facility. The locations selected for storing reserve EVMs and VVPATs are identified and earmarked well in advance of the election day, and this information is formally communicated to all contesting candidates and their agents to ensure mutual trust in the storage arrangements.

The categorization of machines dictates their storage location post-poll. Machines falling under Category C, which are un-polled non-functional units replaced during the mock poll on election day, and Category D, which are unused reserved units, are stored in a strong room separate from the one used for polled machines. These specific storage facilities are guarded by security forces equivalent to half a platoon to ensure their safety. Conversely, all polled EVMs and VVPATs, classified as Category A, along with polled non-functional units classified as Category B, are stored in a dedicated Polled Strong Room. The storage of these voted machines takes place strictly in the presence of candidates or their authorized representatives and is recorded via videography to create an immutable visual record of the sealing and storage process. The Polled Strong Room, containing the actual votes of the electorate, is afforded the highest level of protection, with security forces equivalent to one full platoon deployed to guard the facility until the counting day.

All candidates who are contesting in an election are granted the specific right to appoint their own authorized representatives, often referred to as polling agents, for the express purpose of monitoring the security arrangements surrounding the strong room. A strong room is a highly secured facility designated for the safe storage of voting machines and related electoral materials before and after the polling process. These appointed representatives are permitted to remain stationed immediately outside the inner perimeter of this secure area, ensuring they can observe security protocols without compromising the restricted nature of the interior space. In situations where the physical entrance to the strong room is not directly visible from the location where these agents are standing, the election authorities are required to arrange for a closed-circuit television, commonly known as CCTV, display system. This video feed allows the representatives to visually monitor the entrance and any activities occurring there in real time, thereby maintaining transparency and building trust in the security of the electoral materials even when direct line-of-sight is obstructed.

Regarding the specific procedures for the use of Electronic Voting Machines, abbreviated as EVMs, and Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail units, known as VVPATs, in the event that a re-poll is ordered, strict guidelines must be followed to ensure clarity and prevent confusion. A re-poll occurs when voting at a specific polling station is declared invalid due to irregularities or other issues, necessitating a fresh round of voting. When such a situation arises, the EVMs and VVPATs that are to be used for this new voting session are selected exclusively from the list of unused reserve machines. The selection of these specific machines is formally prepared and the details are communicated in writing to all contesting candidates and their authorized agents to ensure full awareness and accountability. To further distinguish these machines from those used in the original, now-invalidated poll, a clear address tag is affixed to every EVM and VVPAT unit intended for the re-poll. This tag explicitly states that the equipment is designated for use in a re-poll and includes critical identifying information such as the specific date of the re-poll and the unique number of the polling station where it will be deployed.

In addition to the address tags, pre-printed stickers bearing the bold inscription Re-poll EVM or Re-poll VVPAT are physically pasted onto the machines themselves as well as on the carrying cases used to transport them. This dual labeling system serves as a robust visual indicator to election officials, candidates, and observers that these devices are distinct from the original set. Once the re-poll voting process has been completed, the strong room where the polled machines are stored must be reopened. This reopening is a critical security step that must take place in the joint presence of the contesting candidates or their agents and the independent Observer appointed for the constituency. This collective presence ensures that the storage of the newly polled EVMs is witnessed by all stakeholder groups, eliminating any possibility of tampering allegations. Inside the strong room, the EVMs and VVPATs that were just used in the re-poll are placed together with the old EVMs and VVPATs that were utilized during the original poll which was subsequently cancelled.

To manage the counting process effectively and avoid any mix-ups between valid and invalid votes, specific labeling protocols are enacted at the time the re-poll machines are secured in the strong room. Stickers with the bold print TO BE COUNTED are affixed to the EVMs and VVPATs that were used in the re-poll, signaling to the counting staff that the data contained within these machines constitutes the valid votes for that constituency. Conversely, the old EVMs and VVPATs from the original, nullified poll are marked with stickers reading NOT TO BE COUNTED in bold letters. This clear demarcation removes any potential confusion during the vote counting phase, ensuring that only the votes cast during the valid re-poll are tallied while the data from the aborted original poll is strictly excluded. For individuals seeking more comprehensive information regarding the security measures, administrative safeguards, and detailed technical instructions related to EVMs and VVPATs, official resources such as the EVM Manual, the Status Paper on Electronic Voting Machines, various presentations on the technology, and Frequently Asked Questions documents are made available on the website of the Election Commission of India, often abbreviated as ECI.

The maintenance of the secrecy of voting is a fundamental legal requirement enshrined in Section 128 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, which is the primary legislation governing elections in the country. This section mandates that every polling agent, who represents a candidate at the polling station, must actively maintain and aid in maintaining the confidentiality of how each voter casts their ballot. It is strictly prohibited for any polling agent to communicate any information that could potentially violate this secrecy to any other person, whether inside or outside the polling station. The law treats breaches of voting secrecy with significant severity; any person found contravening these provisions is subject to criminal punishment. Upon conviction, the offender may be sentenced to imprisonment for a term that can extend up to three months, or they may be levied a monetary fine, or they may face both imprisonment and a fine simultaneously, depending on the discretion of the court.

Before the actual polling process begins on election day, the Presiding Officer, who is the senior-most election official in charge of the polling station, has a mandatory duty to bring these legal provisions to the attention of everyone present. The Presiding Officer must verbally announce the details of Section 128 of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, clearly explaining the obligation of all personnel and agents to preserve the secrecy of the vote. Furthermore, the officer must explicitly state the penalties associated with any breach of this duty, serving as a formal warning to deter any potential misconduct. Following these preliminary announcements and checks, the Presiding Officer must make a formal declaration using a prescribed format known as Annexure 16, Part I. This declaration serves as an official record confirming that all necessary preliminaries, such as machine checks and seal verifications, have been successfully completed. The Presiding Officer is required to read this declaration aloud so that it is heard by all persons present within the polling station, ensuring transparency.

After reading the declaration, the Presiding Officer signs the document and then proceeds to obtain the signatures of the polling agents who are present and willing to sign. The act of signing this declaration by the agents is crucial as it signifies their collective satisfaction that the poll is commencing in a free and fair manner, without coercion or procedural errors. However, if any polling agent refuses to sign the declaration, the Presiding Officer is not halted from proceeding; instead, the officer must make a specific note of the refusing agent's name in the dedicated paragraph provided for such instances within the declaration form itself. Additionally, before the poll officially starts, the Presiding Officer must obtain the signatures of the candidates or their polling agents on the green paper seal that secures the voting machine. The agents are also allowed to note down the serial numbers of these seals, creating a verifiable chain of custody and ensuring that any tampering with the seals later can be easily detected.

The commencement of the poll is a precisely timed event that occurs exactly at the stroke of the hour fixed for the purpose by the election authorities. By this specific time, the Presiding Officer must have already completed all the aforementioned preliminaries, including the machine checks, declarations, and seal verifications. It is important to note that under no circumstances can the Presiding Officer extend the appointed closing time of the poll, with one specific exception. The only permissible extension is to allow those voters who have already arrived at the polling station before the official closing time and have taken their place in the queue to cast their votes. To manage this exception fairly and prevent new voters from joining the line after the deadline, the Presiding Officer must distribute slips that are duly signed by him to the electors who were physically present at the station at the exact hour appointed for the close of the poll. These slips are distributed starting from the tail end of the queue and moving towards the head, effectively freezing the line and ensuring that no additional voters can enter the queue after the cutoff time.

Immediately before the very first voter signs their name in Form 17A, which serves as the Register of Voters, a critical technical check must be performed. The Polling Officer-I, who assists the Presiding Officer, must verify with the Presiding Officer that the total count displayed on the Control Unit of the Electronic Voting Machine is zero. This check confirms that the machine is fresh and contains no residual data from previous tests or usage. Once verified, this fact must be recorded permanently in ink in Form 17A, stating explicitly that the Total in the Control Unit was checked and found to be Zero. This step provides an auditable baseline proving that the vote counting started from a clean slate. Regarding the entry of voters into the polling station, standard operating procedures dictate that there should normally be separate queues established for men and women voters to ensure order and comfort. The personnel responsible for enforcing these queues, often police or security staff, are instructed to allow only three or four voters to enter the polling station building at any given time, or whatever number the Presiding Officer may specifically direct based on the capacity of the room.

All other voters waiting to enter must stand in an orderly queue outside the building. It is strictly mandated that the formation of more than one queue for men voters or more than one queue for women voters is not allowed, as multiple lines could lead to confusion, crowding, or opportunities for impersonation. The system relies on a single, linear progression for each gender group to maintain control and visibility. Polling agents, despite their role in observing the process, are instructed not to object to this arrangement of single queues and batched entry, as these measures are designed to uphold the integrity and smooth functioning of the polling station. These detailed procedural requirements, from the labeling of machines for re-polls to the management of voter queues and the legal enforcement of voting secrecy, collectively form a comprehensive framework designed to ensure that elections are conducted with the highest standards of security, transparency, and fairness.

To accommodate the practical realities faced by women voters, who often bear significant responsibilities for household chores and family care, election regulations have been established to facilitate their participation in the democratic process. Recognizing that these domestic duties can make waiting in long lines particularly burdensome, the rules permit a specific ratio for entry into polling stations, allowing two women voters to enter for every single male voter. This measure is designed to reduce the time women spend away from their homes while ensuring they can still exercise their franchise. Furthermore, the Presiding Officer, who is the senior official responsible for managing the polling station, has a mandatory duty to prioritize the needs of senior citizens and electors with physical challenges. These vulnerable groups must be granted immediate access to the polling station without being required to wait in the general queue alongside other electors. To operationalize this priority, the Presiding Officer must ensure that all necessary assistance is readily available within the polling station premises. If the volume of such voters warrants it, arrangements should be made to establish a separate, dedicated queue specifically for senior citizens and physically challenged individuals to streamline their entry and minimize physical strain. A critical component of this accessibility mandate is the guarantee that voters using wheelchairs are provided with full facilities to bring their mobility aids inside the polling station, ensuring that architectural barriers do not prevent them from casting their votes. Additionally, to maintain order and fairness in the flow of voters, men and women should generally be admitted into the polling station in alternate batches, creating a structured and balanced entry process.

Regarding the presence of media personnel, specific guidelines govern the activities of press representatives and photographers to balance transparency with the sanctity of the voting process. Subject to the overarching requirement of maintaining peace and public order at the site, there is no objection to photographers capturing images of voters as they line up outside the polling station. This allows for media coverage of voter turnout and civic engagement. However, strict security protocols prohibit anyone, including publicity officials employed by the State Government, from entering the interior of a polling station or a vote-counting center unless they possess a formal letter of authority issued directly by the Election Commission. This restriction ensures that only vetted individuals with explicit permission can observe the sensitive inner workings of the election. Most importantly, under absolutely no circumstances is any photograph allowed to be taken of a voter while they are in the act of recording their vote. This absolute prohibition protects the principle of the secret ballot, ensuring that voters can make their choices free from intimidation, coercion, or the fear that their preference might be exposed to the public or political actors.

The method of recording votes using Electronic Voting Machines, commonly known as EVMs, requires that Polling Agents, who represent the various political candidates, be completely familiar with the operational procedure. This knowledge is essential so that agents do not raise unnecessary or unfounded objections regarding the procedures followed at the polling station, which could otherwise disrupt the voting process. The technical sequence of voting begins only after several procedural requirements are met. First, the identity of the elector must be rigorously established. Once verified, indelible ink, a special stain that cannot be easily washed off, is applied to the elector's left forefinger to prevent multiple voting. Following this, the elector provides a signature or thumb impression on the register. Only after these steps are completed and the elector is authorized to proceed does the Presiding Officer or the Polling Officer in charge of the Control Unit of the voting machine press the button labeled 'Ballot' on the Control Unit. This action activates the system, making the Balloting Unit or units ready to record the specific vote of that elector. When the 'Ballot' button is pressed, a lamp marked 'Busy' on the Control Unit illuminates in red, signaling that the machine is active. Simultaneously, a lamp marked 'Ready' on each Balloting Unit located inside the private voting compartment begins to glow green, indicating to the voter that the machine is prepared to accept their input.

To cast their vote, the elector enters the privacy of the voting compartment and presses the blue button, often referred to as the candidate's button, which is positioned directly against the name, photograph, and symbol of the candidate of their choice. Each candidate contesting the election has a dedicated blue button associated with their specific details on the machine. The moment the elector presses this blue button, the green 'Ready' lamp extinguishes, and a specific lamp located near the pressed button on the Balloting Unit starts glowing red, confirming the selection visually. Concurrently, the Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail, or VVPAT, which serves as a physical verification mechanism, prints a small slip of paper containing the symbol, name, and serial number of the chosen candidate. This slip remains visible behind a transparent window on the VVPAT unit for exactly seven seconds, allowing the voter to verify that their vote was recorded correctly before it drops into a sealed box. At the same time, a distinct 'beep' sound emanates from the Control Unit, audible to all present, providing an audio confirmation of the vote registration. After a few seconds, the red light on the candidate's lamp, the red 'Busy' light on the Control Unit, and the beep sound all cease automatically. These synchronized visual and audio signals serve as the definitive indication that the vote for the selected candidate has been successfully recorded and stored in the memory of the Control Unit. Following this, the Balloting Unit automatically locks itself, preventing any further inputs until the Presiding Officer presses the 'Ballot' button again to reset the machine for the next voter.

In instances where a voter is not properly aware of how to operate the Electronic Voting Machine, the Presiding Officer is authorized to provide a demonstration using a cardboard model of the EVM that is kept at the polling station for training purposes. This ensures that every voter understands the mechanism without compromising the secrecy of the actual vote. It is strictly prohibited for Polling Agents or any other personnel to enter the voting compartment to assist voters, thereby preserving the isolation and confidentiality of the voting act. The overall voting procedure at the polling station follows a streamlined sequence to ensure efficiency and integrity. When an elector first enters the polling station, they proceed directly to the first Polling Officer, who is responsible for verifying the identity of electors against the marked copy of the electoral roll, which is the official list of registered voters. Once the elector's identity is established and no challenges are raised by any of the observing Polling Agents regarding their eligibility, the elector moves to the second stage. Here, a second Polling Officer applies the indelible ink to the elector's left forefinger. Crucially, this inking is performed before the officer makes entries in the Register of Voters, officially known as Form 17A. This specific ordering of tasks ensures that the ink has sufficient time to dry before the voter leaves the polling station, preventing smudging. This same officer is also in charge of maintaining the Register of Voters, where they will note down the elector's serial number as it appears on the electoral roll in the designated column, completing the administrative record of their participation.

In the administrative process of managing voter registration and verification at polling stations, specific columns within Form 17A, which serves as the official Register of Voters, must be completed with precise data regarding the identification documents presented by electors. When an elector arrives to cast their ballot, the polling official is required to enter the last four digits of the identification document into column three of this register. This procedure ensures a concise yet unique reference to the document without cluttering the register with lengthy numerical strings. However, a distinct protocol applies to those electors who are voting based on their Electoral Photo Identity Card, commonly known by the acronym EPIC. For these individuals, it is wholly sufficient for the official to merely inscribe the letters EP in the relevant column, as these letters explicitly denote the use of an EPIC card. In such cases, there is no requirement to write down the full alphanumeric number of the EPIC card itself, thereby streamlining the entry process for the majority of voters who possess this standard identification. Conversely, for those electors who do not possess an EPIC card and instead rely on alternative forms of authorized identification documents, the original instruction remains strictly in force, requiring the recording of the last four digits of whichever alternative document they have produced. Furthermore, the specific type or name of the alternative document presented must also be clearly mentioned in the register to maintain an accurate audit trail of the identification methods used. Following the recording of these identification details, the polling official must secure the signature or, in cases where the elector is unable to sign, the thumb impression of the voter in column four of the said register, directly against the entry corresponding to that specific individual. To reinforce the record-keeping process, the last four digits of the EPIC or any other identity document produced by the elector must also be entered into the Remarks column, which is designated as column five of the Register of Voters Form 17A. Once these entries are correctly made and verified, the second polling officer stationed at the desk will proceed to prepare a voter's slip, a small token or ticket that serves as authorization for the elector to proceed to the actual voting booth.

Upon receiving the voter's slip, the elector moves forward to the next stage of the voting process, approaching either the Presiding Officer or the third Polling Officer, depending on which official is currently assigned the responsibility of managing the Control Unit of the electronic voting machine. Before allowing the vote to be cast, the Presiding Officer or the Third Polling Officer, as applicable to the situation, must conduct a physical inspection of the elector's left forefinger. The primary purpose of this inspection is to verify the presence of an indelible ink mark, a special stain applied to the finger to indicate that the person has already voted, thus preventing multiple voting attempts by the same individual. If the ink mark is clearly visible and intact, the officer will allow the elector to record their vote in the voting machine based on the authority granted by the previously issued voter's slip. The mechanical and procedural steps for actually recording the vote using the voting machine have been detailed in preceding sections of the operational manual, ensuring that the elector knows how to select their preferred candidate securely and privately. It is a fundamental rule of the polling process that electors must be allowed to record their votes in the voting machine in the exact same sequential order in which their names appear in the Register of Voters, Form 17A. This strict adherence to serial order maintains the integrity and traceability of the voting log. However, recognizing that real-world scenarios often present unforeseen challenges, if it becomes impossible to follow this exact serial order for any specific elector due to exceptional circumstances, emergencies, or unavoidable reasons, a corrective measure must be taken. In such instances, a suitable entry detailing the exact serial number at which the elector actually voted must be recorded in the remarks column of the register against that person's name. Furthermore, similar explanatory entries must be made for all subsequent voters whose position in the serial order has been disturbed or shifted as a result of this deviation, ensuring that the final account of the day's voting activity can be fully reconciled.

To further safeguard the integrity of the election, the task of checking the left forefinger of a voter just before they exit the polling station may be delegated by the Presiding Officer to another trusted member of their polling party. This secondary check serves as a final quality control measure to ensure that every departing voter bears a clear and visible indelible ink mark.
